Second and Third Proofs Against the Philosophers' Definition of Body
Second and third arguments against capability as part of body's quiddity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical arguments that capability for dimensions cannot be a constitutive part of the quiddity of body, and why this invalidates the philosophical definition as a proper ḥadd.

Core Idea
Capability for dimensions is a judgment that presupposes the essence, so it cannot define it.

The philosophers defined body as 'that which is capable of receiving three dimensions.' But is this a proper definition? In this passage, the author presents two additional arguments—the second and third faces—to show that such capability is external to the essence of body. Let's examine these logical proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the structure: second face, third face, then a conclusion referencing three faces.
The passage is concise; each face is a single argument.
2
Understand the second face argument
The second face argues that capability is a judgment (ḥukm) that occurs after the complete essence of body. Since the essence must exist first, capability cannot be a part of the quiddity.
3
Understand the third face argument
The third face argues that capability is a relative, additional attribute, while the essence is self-subsistent. An additional attribute cannot constitute a non-additional essence.
4
Connect both to the conclusion
Both arguments, together with the first face (from a previous lesson), establish that capability is external to quiddity. Therefore, the definition cannot be a proper ḥadd.
5
Reflect on the significance
This shows that definitions must capture essential, not accidental, attributes. Capability is an accident that depends on the essence.
This guards against a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في بيان أن كون الجسم قابلا للأبعاد الثلاثة يمتنع أن يكون جزءاً من ماهية الجسم : هو أن كون الجسم قابلاً لكذا ، وكذا : حكم . إنما يحصل بعد تمام ذات الجسم. فإنه ما لم توجد ذات الجسم ، امتنع أن يكون قابلا لشيء آخر . فثبت : أن هذه القابلية خارجة عن تلك الماهية. الوجه الثالث : إن كون الجسم قابلاً لكذا : صفة نسبية إضافية . وذات الجسم : ذات قائم بالنفس . والأمر الإضافي يمتنع كونه مقوماً للأمر الذي لا يكون إضافياً. فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : أن كون الجسم قابلاً للأبعاد الثلاثة لا يمكن أن يكون جزءاً داخلاً في ماهية الجسم. وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع كون هذا التعريف حدا للجسم
The second aspect (al-wajh al-thānī) in explaining that the body's being capable of the three dimensions cannot be a part of the quiddity of body: that the body's being capable of such and such is a judgment; it only occurs after the complete essence of the body. For as long as the essence of the body does not exist, it is impossible for it to be capable of anything else. So it is established that this capability is external to that quiddity. The third aspect: The body's being capable of such and such is a relative, additional attribute. The essence of the body is a self-subsistent essence. And it is impossible for an additional thing to be constitutive of something that is not additional. So it is established by these three aspects that the body's being capable of the three dimensions cannot be an internal part of the quiddity of the body. And if that is so, it is impossible for this definition to be a definition (ḥadd) of body.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents the second and third faces (wajh) of the argument that the philosophers' definition of body as 'that which is capable of receiving three dimensions' cannot serve as a proper definition (ḥadd). The second face argues that capability for dimensions is a judgment (ḥukm) that occurs only after the complete existence of the body's essence (dhāt). Since the essence must first exist before it can have capabilities, capability cannot be a constituent part of the essence. Thus, capability is external to the quiddity. The third face argues that capability is a relative, additional attribute (ṣifa nisbiyya iḍāfiyya), while the essence of body is a self-subsistent essence (dhāt qāʾimun bi-l-nafs). An additional (iḍāfī) thing cannot be constitutive (muqawwim) of something that is not additional. Therefore, capability cannot be part of the quiddity. The conclusion (thubita) from all three faces is that capability for three dimensions cannot be an internal part of the quiddity, so the definition cannot be a ḥadd.

Key Terms
ذات (dhāt)
حكم (ḥukm)
صفة نسبية إضافية (ṣifa nisbiyya iḍāfiyya)
مقوم (muqawwim)
قائم بالنفس (qāʾim bi-l-nafs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Capability for dimensions is an essential part of the definition of body.
Correction: The author argues that capability is a judgment and relative attribute that presupposes the essence, so it cannot be constitutive (muqawwim) of the quiddity.

Checkpoint
What does the second face (al-wajh al-thānī) argue about capability for dimensions?
A) It is a constitutive part of the quiddity of body.
B) It is a judgment that occurs after the complete essence of the body.
C) It is an existent attribute that causes infinite regress.
D) It is the same as the body's form.

Key Takeaway
The capability for dimensions cannot be part of the quiddity of body because it is a judgment and relative attribute that presupposes the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that a judgment cannot define the essence it presupposes. Where in your own life have you mistaken a consequence or a property of your identity for its very core? What would it mean to stop doing that?

First Two Proofs: Potentiality is a Non-Existent Attribute
Two proofs showing potentiality is a non-existent attribute, not part of body's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first two proofs establish that potentiality to receive dimensions is a non-existent attribute, not part of the body's essence.

Core Idea
Potentiality cannot be an existing attribute because it leads to infinite regress or requires an attribute in non-existence.

The author begins by tackling the claim that the specific potentiality to receive three dimensions is an existing attribute. Two arguments show its impossibility: the first leads to an infinite regress, the second would require an attribute to inhere in nothing. Both conclude that potentiality is non-existent and cannot be part of the body's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the first two proofs
Read the Arabic passage containing the first and second proofs. Notice how each proof starts with a conditional and ends with an impossibility.
The first proof uses the concept of 'suitability of locus'.
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
If potentiality were an existing attribute inhering in a locus, that locus must itself have a potentiality to receive it, leading to an infinite chain. This is impossible.
3
Understand the second argument about existence from non-existence
The specificity of potentiality is an attribute of the origin of potentiality, which is non-existent. If it were existent, it would inhere in non-existence, which is impossible.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Both proofs show potentiality is non-existent; thus it cannot be a part of the essence of an existing body.
This refutes the idea that potentiality is part of the essential definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان الأول: إنه لو كان مسمى القابلية أمراً موجوداً [ القابلية المخصوصة (۱) ] لكان صفة قائمة بمحل (۲) فكانت قابلية المحل [ لها (٣) ] زائدة (٤) عليها . ويلزم التسلسل . وبيان الثاني : إن تلك الخصوصية صفة الأصل (1) القابلية ، فلو كانت هذه الخصوصية صفة موجودة ، مع أنها صفة الأصل القابلية (٥) لزم قيام الموجود بالمعدوم . وهو محال . فثبت : أن هذه القابلية المخصوصة صفة عدمية ، والصفة العلمية يمتنع كونها جزءاً من أجزاء ماهية الجسم الموجود . الوجه الثاني في بيان أن كون الجسم قابلا للأبعاد الثلاثة يمتنع أن يكون جزءاً من ماهية الجسم : هو أن كون الجسم قابلاً لكذا ، وكذا : حكم . إنما يحصل بعد تمام ذات الجسم. فإنه ما لم توجد ذات الجسم ، امتنع أن يكون قابلا لشيء آخر . قثبت : أن هذه القابلية خارجة عن تلك الماهية . الوجه الثالث : إن كون الجسم قابلاً لكذا : صفة نسبية إضافية . وذات الجسم : ذات قائم بالنفس . والأمر الإضافي يمتنع كونه مقومـاً للأمر الذي لا يكون إضافياً . فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : أن كون الجسم قابلاً للأبعاد الثلاثة لا يمكن أن يكون جزءاً داخلاً في ماهية الجسم. وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع كون هذا التعريف حدا للجسم
Statement of the first: If the meaning of potentiality were an existent thing [the specific potentiality (1)], it would be an attribute subsisting in a locus (2), so the suitability of the locus for it (3) would be additional (4) to it, and regress would follow. And statement of the second: That specificity is an attribute of the origin (1) of potentiality; so if this specificity were an existent attribute, while it is an attribute of the origin of potentiality (5), the existence of the existent through the non-existent would necessarily follow, which is impossible. So it is established that this specific potentiality is a non-existent attribute, and it is impossible for an attribute of non-existence to be part of the parts of the essence of the existing body. The second aspect in explaining that it is impossible for the body’s being capable of three dimensions to be a part of the essence of the body: It is that the body’s being capable of such-and-such is a judgment that occurs only after the complete completion of the essence of the body. For as long as the essence of the body does not exist, it is impossible for it to be capable of something else. So it is established that this potentiality is external to that essence. The third aspect: The body’s being capable of such-and-such is a relative, attributive quality. The essence of the body is a self-subsisting essence. An attributive thing cannot be a constituent of something that is not attributive. So it is established by these three aspects: that the body’s being capable of three dimensions cannot be an internal part of the essence of the body. And if so, it is impossible for this definition to be a definition (had, essential definition) of the body.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs (wujuh) that the definition of body as 'that which is capable of receiving three dimensions' cannot be an essential definition (had). The first proof shows that if potentiality were an existing attribute, it would require another potentiality for its own realization, leading to infinite regress. The second proof argues that the specificity of potentiality would be an attribute of the origin of potentiality, which is non-existent, thus requiring the impossible: an attribute inhering in nothing. The third proof notes that potentiality is a relational attribute, but the essence of the body is non-relational and self-subsistent; a relational attribute cannot be a constituent of a non-relational essence. The conclusion states that all three proofs prove the definition cannot be a had (essential definition) of body. The passage follows a logical, step-by-step refutation format typical of kalam arguments.

Key Terms
قابلي (potentiality)
صفة عدمية (non-existent attribute)
تسلسل (regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think potentiality is an existing entity that is part of the body's definition.
Correction: The passage shows that potentiality is a non-existent attribute (ʿadamī) and thus cannot be a part of the essence of an existing body.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the two proofs in this passage?
A) Potentiality is an existing attribute and part of the body's essence.
B) Potentiality is a non-existent attribute and cannot be part of the body's essence.
C) Potentiality is a self-subsistent entity.
D) Potentiality is irrelevant to the definition of body.

Key Takeaway
The first two proofs demonstrate that potentiality is a non-existent attribute, so it cannot be an internal part of the body's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If potentiality is non-existent, what does that say about your own potential to change? Are you using the fact that it's non-existent as an excuse to remain as you are?
?
Consider the infinite regress argument: every potentiality requires another potentiality. How does this expose your tendency to keep putting off surrender to Allah, thinking you need more preparation?
?
The proof shows that a non-existent attribute cannot be part of your essence. Does your identity rest on what you could become rather than what you are before Allah right now? How can you turn from imagined futures to the present reality of your servitude?

Second Aspect: Potentiality is a Judgment After Essence
Proof that potentiality is a judgment after the essence, so external.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why potentiality must be external to the body's essence because it is a judgment that occurs after the essence is complete.

Core Idea
Potentiality to receive dimensions is a judgment that only applies after the body's essence exists, so it is external.

The second aspect (wajh) offers a different line of reasoning: the property of being capable of receiving three dimensions is a judgment (ḥukm) that can only be attributed to the body after its essence is fully realized. Since the essence must exist before such a judgment, the potentiality cannot be part of the essence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the second aspect passage
Read the Arabic text of the second aspect. Notice how it defines potentiality as a 'judgment' (ḥukm).
2
Understand the timing argument
The judgment of being capable only occurs after the complete existence of the essence. Therefore it is posterior to the essence.
This shows potentiality is an accident (ʿaraḍ) not a constituent.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Since potentiality is posterior, it cannot be an internal part (juzʾ dākhil) of the essence. It is external.

?

?

In your daily life, where do you rely on your own inherent capacity rather than on Allah's constant granting of that judgment? What would change if you saw each moment of capability as a fresh judgment from Allah?
?

Are you conscious of your absolute dependence on Allah for every act, even the smallest? Where in your life do you drift into the illusion that your potential is self-sufficient, and how can you remind yourself of this lesson's truth?

Third Aspect: Relational Attribute Cannot Constituent a Non-Relational Essence
Proof that relational attribute cannot constitute non-relational essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a relational attribute like potentiality cannot be a constituent part of a non-relational essence like body.

Core Idea
A relational attribute cannot be a constituent of a self-subsistent, non-relational essence.

The third and final proof draws on the nature of relational attributes. Body's essence is self-subsistent (qāʾim bi al-nafs), not relational. But potentiality to receive dimensions is a relative, attributive quality (ṣifa nisbiyya iḍāfiyya). Since a constituent of something must share its nature, a relational item cannot be a constituent of a non-relational essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the third aspect passage
Read the Arabic text. Note the contrast between 'relational attributive quality' and 'self-subsisting essence'.
2
Understand the incompatibility
Something relational depends on others, while the body's essence is independent. A relational item cannot be a fundamental part (muqawwim) of something non-relational.
This is a conceptual argument about categories.
3
See the final conclusion
All three aspects together prove that potentiality cannot be an internal part of the body's essence, so the definition is not a genuine essential definition (ḥadd).

Source Text

First Proof: Body as Form Cannot Be Potential
The author argues that body is form (actuality) while potentiality belongs to matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of body as 'capable of receiving three dimensions' is rejected, because it confuses form (actuality) with matter (potentiality).

Core Idea
Body is actual form, not potential matter — so it cannot be the receiver of dimensions.

We now examine the first proof against a proposed definition of body. The argument draws a fundamental distinction between form, which is actual, and matter, which is potential. The definition in question, it claims, actually describes matter, not the body itself. This short but crucial argument sets the stage for the correct understanding of what a material substance is.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: premise, reasoning, conclusion.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key distinction
The passage contrasts form (sura) and matter (hayula). Form is what makes a thing actual; matter is what receives dimensions. Understand this distinction before moving on.
This distinction is central to Islamic philosophy.
3
Follow the logical argument
The argument reasons: body is form; form is actual; actual cannot be potential; therefore body cannot be a receiver. The receiver must be matter. Hence the definition fails.
Pay attention to the phrase 'one thing cannot be a cause for both potentiality and actuality together.'
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: why does this matter for defining 'body'? What does it tell us about the nature of material substances?
This refines our understanding of what a body is philosophically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان لا يمكن كونه رسمياً لماهية الجسم . فلوجوه : الأول : إن الجسمية عندهم صورة . والصورة هي [ الجزء (1) ] الذي به يكون الشيء بالفعل [ وما كان كذلك امتنع كونه قابلاً لشيء آخر ، لأن عندهم الشيء الواحد لا يكون سبباً للقوة وللفعل معا (1) ] ولما كان ذلك كذلك ، كان القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة : ليس إلا الهيولى فهذا الذي جعلوه معرفاً للجسم . لم يصدق البتة على الجسم ، وإنما صدق على هيولى الجسم ، فكان باطلاً .
And as for the explanation that it cannot be a definition by property (rasm) for the essence of the body, there are several aspects: The first: Bodily nature according to them is form. And form is the part by which a thing is actual. And if it is like that, it is impossible for it to be capable of something else, because according to them one thing cannot be a cause for both potentiality and actuality together. And since that is the case, the receiver of the three dimensions is only the matter (hayula). So this which they made as a definition of the body does not apply to the body at all, but applies to the matter of the body, so it is false.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage begins the second part of the argument, showing that the definition cannot even be a rasm (definition by accidental properties) for the body. The first proof states that body (jawhara) according to the philosophers is a form (sura), that is, something actual (bil-fi‘l). A form, being actual, cannot simultaneously be potential; one thing cannot be a cause for both potency and act. The receiver of three dimensions is the hyle (matter), not form. Therefore the definition of body as 'capable of receiving three dimensions' actually defines matter, not the body itself, making it false.

صورة (form)
هيولى (matter/hyle)

فعل (actuality)
جسمية (bodily nature)

Misconception: The definition 'capable of receiving three dimensions' accurately describes a body.
Correction: The passage argues that this definition actually describes matter, because reception implies potentiality, whereas body is form and is actual.

Checkpoint

A) The form of the body
B) The matter of the body
C) The body itself
D) The essence of the body

Key Takeaway
The definition of body as a receiver of dimensions actually defines matter, because reception implies potentiality, while body as form is actual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You call yourself by labels like 'student' or 'beginner'—do you see these as your actual form (what you are) or your potential (what you could become)? If you treat yourself as potential only, will you ever act?
?
The philosophers argued one thing cannot be both potential and actual. In your life, are you trying to hold onto what you could do while refusing to actually do it? What is one area where you must choose actuality today?
?
If body is defined by its actuality, not its receptivity, then your identity is defined by what you are, not what you can receive. Are you living out who you are, or are you letting circumstances define you? How will you reclaim your actual self today?

Body is Intuitively Known: No Need for Definition
The author argues that body is known by necessity and cannot be defined by a rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the body cannot be defined by a rasm (definition by property) and why intuitive knowledge makes such definition unnecessary.

Core Idea
Body is known by necessity; definitions are only for unknown essences.

This lesson examines a key methodological point in Islamic kalam: some things are too obvious to define. The author argues that body is exactly such a case—it is intuitively known by every rational mind. This passage shows how the nature of knowledge determines whether a definition is valid or redundant.

Guided Path
1
Understand the purpose of a rasm
Read the passage's opening sentence: The author defines rasm as used when the essence is unknown in itself. Understand that a rasm is a definition by distinctive properties, not essence.
This sets the condition for when definition is needed.
2
Read the argument carefully
Now read the rest of the passage. Notice the claim: body is known necessarily (ma'lum bi al-darura) and every rational person conceives it intuitively.
Pay attention to the examples: motion, rest, colors, tastes.
3
Compare body with other existents
The author says body is distinguished from other existents by intuition. Ask yourself: Do I need a definition to know what body is? The argument says no.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since the conception of body is present in all minds, it is impossible (and pointless) to define it by something else. Understand why this constitutes a proof against the need for a definition of body.
This conclusion refutes the notion that body needs a formal definition.

الثاني : إن الرسم عبارة عما إذا كانت الماهية مجهولة في نفسها ، فنعرفها بصفة معلومة . وليس الأمر ههنا كذلك. لأن الجسم أعني هذا الشيء الذي له حجم ومقدار أمر معلوم بالضرورة، وكل عاقل فإنه ببديهة عقله يتصوره ويتعرفه ، ويميز بينه وبين سائر الموجودات . مثل : الحركة والسكون ، والألوان والطعوم ، وغيرها . وإذا كان تصور هذه الماهية حاصلاً في جمع العقول والأفهام ، امتنع تعريفها بشيء آخر
The second: A definition by property (rasm) is used when the essence is unknown in itself, so we define it by a known attribute. But this is not the case here. Because the body, I mean this thing that has volume and magnitude, is known necessarily, and every rational person by the intuition of his mind conceives it and recognizes it, and distinguishes it from other existents, such as motion and rest, colors and tastes, and others. And since the conception of this essence is present in all minds and understandings, it is impossible to define it by something else.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage gives the second proof against the definition as a rasm. It states that a rasm is used when the essence (māhiyya) is unknown in itself; then we define it via a known attribute. But body (jism) is known necessarily (bi al-ḍarūra) to every rational person; everyone intuitively distinguishes it from other things like motion, color, etc. Since its conception is already present in all minds, it is impossible and useless to define it by something else. The implication is that the proposed definition is unnecessary and redundant.

ماهية (essence)
معلوم بالضرورة (known necessarily)
بديهة (intuition)

Misconception: One might think that body needs a formal definition (like 'substance with extension') in order to be understood.
Correction: This argument shows that body is self-evident and known by necessity; no definition is needed because all rational minds already grasp it intuitively.

Checkpoint

B) Because body is known necessarily and intuitively

D) Because the proposed definition is too simple

Key Takeaway
Body is intuitively known by all rational minds, so defining it by a rasm is impossible and unnecessary.

Third Proof: The Definition is More Obscure Than Body
The author argues that the proposed definition is more obscure than body itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining 'body' by its capacity for three dimensions is logically flawed and violates a fundamental principle of definition in logic.

Core Idea
The clear should never be defined by the obscure.

In this lesson, the author presents his third argument against a proposed definition of body. He contends that the definition (something receptive to three dimensions) is more obscure than the thing being defined (body as an extended substance), thereby breaking a crucial logical rule. Let's examine the reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the full Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the compact argumentative style.
Pay attention to the terms 'clear' (جلي) and 'obscure' (خفي).
2
Identify the two concepts being compared
The author compares the essence of body (the thing that occupies space) to the notion of being receptive to three intersecting dimensions. Which one is more familiar to the mind?
The author claims the essence of body is clearer.
3
Understand the logical principle invoked
The author invokes a rule from logic: it is prohibited to define what is clear and apparent by what is obscure and hidden. How does this apply here?
The definition fails because it tries to explain something obvious via something technical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن ذات الجسم - أعني هذا الشيء المتحيز أقرب إلى الأفهام والعقول من كونه قابلاً لفرض الأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة على الزوايا القوائم . فإن جميع العقلاء يتصورون ماهية الحجم والمقدار، ولا يعرفون البتة معنى كونه قابلاً للأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة على الزوايا القوائم ، إلا بتدقيق النظر ، وغامض الفكر ، وتعريف الظاهر الجلي، بالغامض الخفي : منهي عنه في المنطق .
The third: The essence of body – I mean this thing that occupies space (mutahayyiz) – is closer to minds and intellects than its being capable of supposing the three dimensions intersecting at right angles. For all rational people conceive the essence of volume and magnitude, but they do not know at all the meaning of being capable of the three dimensions intersecting at right angles except by close examination and deep thought. And defining the clear and apparent by the obscure and hidden is prohibited in logic.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument: the essence of body (the spatially extended thing) is more apparent to the intellect than the concept of being receptive to three perpendicular dimensions. Everyone understands volume and magnitude, but the notion of accepting three mutually perpendicular dimensions is technical and requires careful thought. Therefore, this definition violates a basic rule of logic: the clear and obvious should not be defined by the obscure and hidden.

Key Terms

زوايا قوائم (right angles)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that technical definitions are always acceptable as long as they are accurate.
Correction: Logic prohibits defining clear and obvious concepts by obscure and technical ones, as this makes the definition less helpful.

Key Takeaway
This argument demonstrates that a sound definition must not be more obscure than the term it defines, otherwise it violates logical norms.

Fourth Proof: Circularity in the Definition
Geometric illustration of circularity in body's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against defining body as 'receptive to three perpendicular dimensions' and why it is circular.

Core Idea
Defining body by its dimensions already assumes you know what a body is.

This lesson examines a classic objection to the definition of body as 'receptive to three perpendicular dimensions.' The author argues that understanding this definition actually requires prior knowledge of body itself. Through a geometric illustration, the circularity becomes clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it begins by stating the circularity claim, then provides a geometric example.
The passage is incomplete, but the main idea is preserved.
2
Understand the geometric example
The author uses lines and right angles to show that constructing three mutually perpendicular dimensions requires imagining a three-dimensional space, i.e., a body.
Think of building a cube from lines – you need the concept of volume.
3
Connect the example to circularity
Since the definition uses 'three dimensions,' but grasping dimensions requires a body, the definition is circular: body explains body.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this objection challenges the definition. Does it apply to all definitions?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن تصور قبول الأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة ، على الزوايا القوائم ، مشروط بتصور ماهية الجسم. وذلك لأنا إذا مددنا خطاً ، ثم أقمنا عليه خطاً آخر ، فإنه يحصل في السطح زاويتان قائمتان فقط ، ويمتنع حصول الزوايا الثلاثة المتقاطعة على القوائم فيه . أما إذا فرضنا قيام خط على [ طرف (٤) ] خط آخر ، فإنه تحصل فيه زاوية واحدة قائمة . ثم إذا فرضنا نزول خط آخر من نقطة التقاطع في العمق ، فإنه يحدث في العمق زاويتان قائمتان . فالعقل ما لم
The fourth: The conception of the acceptance of the three dimensions intersecting at right angles is conditioned by the conception of the essence of the body. This is because if we extend a line, then erect another line upon it, then on the surface only two right angles occur, and it is impossible for three right angles to occur in it. But if we suppose the erection of a line on the end of another line, then one right angle occurs in it. Then if we suppose the descent of another line from the point of intersection in depth, then it produces in depth two right angles. So the mind, unless ...
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage begins the fourth argument: understanding the definition of body as 'receptive to three perpendicular dimensions' already presupposes an understanding of body itself. The author illustrates with geometry: constructing three right angles in three dimensions requires imagining a three-dimensional space, i.e., a body. Without the prior concept of a three-dimensional extension (body), one cannot conceive of three mutually perpendicular lines meeting at right angles. Thus the definition is circular. The passage is incomplete but the reasoning is clear.

Key Terms
قبول (acceptance/receptivity)
أبعاد ثلاثية (three dimensions)
زوايا قوائم (right angles)
تقاطع (intersection)
مشروط (conditional)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The geometric construction can be understood without any reference to a body.
Correction: The construction of three perpendicular lines in depth already assumes a three-dimensional space, which is the very concept of body.

Checkpoint

A) A psychological argument
B) A geometric example with lines and angles
C) An appeal to authority
D) A definition of matter

Key Takeaway
The definition of body as 'receptive to three dimensions' is circular because it already presupposes the concept of body.

First Argument: Confusing Body with Hyle
First argument: confusion with hyle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining body as 'receptive of three dimensions' actually describes hyle (prime matter) and not body itself.

Core Idea
A thing cannot be both actual and receptive; body is actual, so receptivity belongs to hyle.

The first argument targets the core of the definition: it confuses the subject. The philosophers hold that a thing in actuality cannot be receptive of something else simultaneously. Since body is actual, the receptive principle is hyle, not body. This lesson examines that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: premise, conclusion, evaluation.
The argument is concise; each word matters.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument relies on the principle that one thing cannot be both actual and potential. Identify where this is stated and how it applies.
3
Understand the conclusion
The receptivity to dimensions belongs only to hyle, so the definition misses its target. Ask yourself: why does this make the definition false?
The author uses 'batil' (false) to dismiss the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون الشيء بالفعل وما كان كذلك امتنع كونه قابلاً لشيء آخر، لأن عندهم الشيء الواحد لا يكون سبباً للقوة وللفعل معا. ولما كان ذلك كذلك، كان القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة ليس إلا الهيولى فهذا الذي جعلوه معرفاً للجسم. لم يصدق البتة على الجسم، وإنما صدق على هيولى الجسم، فكان باطلاً. الثاني: إن الرسم عبارة عما إذا كانت الماهية مجهولة في نفسها، فنعرفها بصفة معلومة. وليس الأمر ههنا كذلك. لأن الجسم أعني هذا الشيء الذي له حجم ومقدار أمر معلوم بالضرورة، وكل عاقل فإنه ببديهة عقله يتصوره ويتعرفه، ويميز بينه وبين سائر الموجودات. مثل الحركة والسكون، والألوان والطعوم، وغيرها. وإذا كان تصور هذه الماهية حاصلاً في جمع العقول والأفهام، امتنع تعريفها بشيء آخر. الثالث: إن ذات الجسم أعني هذا الشيء المتحيز أقرب إلى الأفهام والعقول من كونه قابلاً لفرض الأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة على الزوايا القوائم. فإن جميع العقلاء يتصورون ماهية الحجم والمقدار، ولا يعرفون البتة معنى كونه قابلاً للأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة على الزوايا القوائم، إلا بتدقيق النظر، وغامض الفكر، وتعريف الظاهر الجلي، بالغامض الخفي منهي عنه في المنطق. الرابع: إن تصور قبول الأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة على الزوايا القوائم مشروط بتصور ماهية الجسم. وذلك لأنا إذا مددنا خطاً، ثم أقمنا عليه خطاً آخر، فإنه يحصل في السطح زاويتان قائمتان فقط، ويمتنع حصول الزوايا الثلاثة المتقاطعة على القوائم فيه. أما إذا فرضنا قيام خط على طرف خط آخر، فإنه تحصل فيه زاوية واحدة قائمة. ثم إذا فرضنا نزول خط آخر من نقطة التقاطع في العمق، فإنه يحدث في العمق زاويتان قائمتان. فالعقل ما لم يتصور العمق والثخن، فإنه لا يمكنه البتة تصور كيفية حصول هذه الزوايا الثلاثة المتقاطعة على القوائم. فيثبت أن تصور هذه الخاصية مشروط بسبق تصور ماهية الجسم. فلو عرفنا ماهية الجسم بهذه الخاصية لزم تعريف الشيء بما لا يعرف إلا به. وذلك باطل، ومنهي عنه في المنطق. الخامس: إن بتقدير أن يكون الحق هو أن الجسم مركب من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ، كان الطول عبارة عن تلك الأجزاء المتألفة في سمت واجد، والسطح عبارة عن تلك الأجزاء المتألفة في سمت الطول والعرض معاً، وعلى هذا التقدير فالطول والعرض والعمق ذوات قائمة بأنفسها، لا صفات قائمة بالغير. فيمتنع الحكم بكونها أموراً مقبولة القابل، ونعوتاً لذات أخرى. فيثبت بهذه الوجوه أن هذا التعريف باطل. وإنما الصواب أن يقال الجسم عبارة عن هذا الحجم، وعن هذا الثخن. ثم يقال من خواص هذه الذوات أنه يمكن أن يحصل فيها زوايا ثلاثة قائمة متقاطعة على نقطة واحدة. فجعل هذه الصفة خاصية من خواص الجسم جائز. أما جعلها معرفة لماهيته، فذلك باطل على ما قررناه
A thing is in actuality, and whatever is like that is prevented from being receptive of something else, because according to them one thing cannot be a cause for both potentiality and actuality together. And since that is the case, the recipient of the three dimensions is only the hyle (hayula). So this is what they made a definition (mu'arraf) of body (jism). It does not apply at all to body, but rather applies to the hyle of body, so it is false. The second: [The point] that a description (rasm) is [used] when the quiddity (mahiyya) is unknown in itself, so we know it by a known attribute. But the matter here is not like that. Because the body—I mean this thing that has volume (hajm) and magnitude (miqdar)—is known necessarily, and every rational person by the intuition of his intellect conceives it and recognizes it, and distinguishes between it and other existents, such as motion and rest, colors and tastes, and others. And if the conception of this quiddity is obtained in all minds and understandings, it is impossible to define it by something else. The third: The essence (dhat) of body—I mean this extended thing (mutahayyiz)—is closer to minds and intellects than its being receptive to the positing of three dimensions intersecting at right angles. For all rational people conceive the quiddity of volume and magnitude, and they do not know at all the meaning of its being receptive to three dimensions intersecting at right angles, except by precise investigation and obscure thought, and defining the manifest and clear by the obscure and hidden is prohibited in logic. The fourth: The conception of the receptivity of three dimensions intersecting at right angles is conditioned upon the prior conception of the quiddity of body. That is because if we extend a line, then erect another line upon it, only two right angles occur in the surface, and it is impossible for the three angles intersecting at right angles to occur in it. But if we suppose a line standing on the extremity of another line, then one right angle occurs in it. Then if we suppose another line descending from the point of intersection into depth, then two right angles occur in depth. So the intellect, as long as it does not conceive depth and thickness, cannot at all conceive the manner of occurrence of these three angles intersecting at right angles. Thus it is established that the conception of this property is conditioned upon prior conception of the quiddity of body. So if we defined the quiddity of body by this property, it would necessitate defining a thing by that which is only known through it. That is false and prohibited in logic. The fifth: Assuming that the truth is that body is composed of indivisible parts (ajza' allati la tatajazzau), then length would consist of those parts arranged in one direction, and surface would consist of those parts arranged in the direction of length and width together. On this assumption, length, width, and depth are self-subsistent essences, not attributes subsisting in another. So it is impossible to judge them as things received by a recipient and as attributes of another essence. Thus by these [five] ways it is established that this definition is false. The correct thing is to say: body is this volume and this thickness. Then it is said: among the properties of these essences is that three right angles intersecting at one point can occur in them. So making this attribute a property (khaassiyya) of body is permissible. But making it a definition of its quiddity is false, as we have established.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the definition of body (jism) as 'that which is receptive of three dimensions intersecting at right angles.' The author presents five arguments against this definition. The first argument: The definition confuses body with prime matter (hayula). According to the philosophers, a thing in actuality cannot be receptive of something else, because one thing cannot be both potential and actual. Since body is actual, the receptive principle is hyle, not body. So the definition applies to hyle, not body. The second argument: A definition by description (rasm) is used when the quiddity (mahiyya) is unknown. But body is known necessarily to all rational people; they conceive its volume and magnitude intuitively. Hence defining it by something else is unnecessary and impossible. The third argument: The essence of body (the extended thing) is more immediately understood than the concept of receptivity to three dimensions. The latter requires precise investigation, while the former is clear. Defining the manifest by the obscure is forbidden in logic. The fourth argument: The conception of receptivity to three dimensions presupposes the conception of body. The author gives a geometric explanation: to conceive three right angles intersecting at a point, one must first conceive depth and thickness, which are aspects of body. Therefore, defining body by that property is circular—defining a thing by something that depends on it. The fifth argument: If body is composed of indivisible parts (the atomist view), then length, width, and depth are self-subsistent entities, not attributes of another. Thus they cannot be received by a subject. Therefore, the definition fails on both the Aristotelian and atomist views. The correct approach is to define body as volume and thickness, and treat receptivity to three dimensions as a property, not a definition.

Key Terms
جسم (jism)
هيولى (hayula)
قوة (quwwa)
فعل (fi'l)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that defining body as 'receptive of three dimensions' captures its essence because dimensions are essential to body.
Correction: The argument shows that receptivity is a property of hyle (prime matter), not of body itself, since body is already in actuality.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why does the definition 'receptive of three dimensions' not apply to body?
A) Body is too complex to have dimensions.
B) Body is in actuality, so it cannot be receptive.
C) The definition confuses body with its accidents.
D) Body is composed of indivisible parts.

Key Takeaway
The definition of body as 'receptive of three dimensions' fails because it describes hyle, not body itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at yourself or others, do you tend to define by outward actions (actual) rather than inner potential? How does this lesson's distinction between body and hyle challenge you to see beyond the surface? Will you commit today to look for the receptive potential in someone you've judged by their current state?
?
Are there areas in your life where you confuse what you are now with what you could become? How can remembering that your 'body' (your manifested deeds) is not your full story—and that your 'hyle' (your soul's potential) is receptive to transformation—change the way you approach your next spiritual goal?

Second Argument: Body is Known by Necessity
Second argument: body known necessarily

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining body by a description (rasm) is impossible since its quiddity is known necessarily to all rational people.

Core Idea
A definition by description is only for unknown quiddities; body is known intuitively to everyone.

The second argument appeals to common sense: everyone already knows what a body is—it has volume and magnitude. A definition by description (rasm) is used when the quiddity is unknown, but body is not unknown. This lesson unpacks that logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English. Note the definition of 'rasm' and how the author applies it to body.
2
Understand the concept of rasm
Rasm is a definition that uses known attributes to clarify an unknown quiddity. Identify where the author explains this.
The author says 'if the quiddity is unknown in itself, we know it by a known attribute.'
3
Apply to body
The author argues that body's quiddity is known necessarily to all rational people, so defining it is impossible. Ask yourself: is it true that everyone knows what a body is?
Consider everyday examples like rocks, water, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون الشيء بالفعل وما كان كذلك امتنع كونه قابلاً لشيء آخر، لأن عندهم الشيء الواحد لا يكون سبباً للقوة وللفعل معا. ولما كان ذلك كذلك، كان القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة ليس إلا الهيولى فهذا الذي جعلوه معرفاً للجسم. لم يصدق البتة على الجسم، وإنما صدق على هيولى الجسم، فكان باطلاً. الثاني: إن الرسم عبارة عما إذا كانت الماهية مجهولة في نفسها، فنعرفها بصفة معلومة. وليس الأمر ههنا كذلك. لأن الجسم أعني هذا الشيء الذي له حجم ومقدار أمر معلوم بالضرورة، وكل عاقل فإنه ببديهة عقله يتصوره ويتعرفه، ويميز بينه وبين سائر الموجودات. مثل الحركة والسكون، والألوان والطعوم، وغيرها. وإذا كان تصور هذه الماهية حاصلاً في جمع العقول والأفهام، امتنع تعريفها بشيء آخر. الثالث: إن ذات الجسم أعني هذا الشيء المتحيز أقرب إلى الأفهام والعقول من كونه قابلاً لفرض الأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة على الزوايا القوائم. فإن جميع العقلاء يتصورون ماهية الحجم والمقدار، ولا يعرفون البتة معنى كونه قابلاً للأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة على الزوايا القوائم، إلا بتدقيق النظر، وغامض الفكر، وتعريف الظاهر الجلي، بالغامض الخفي منهي عنه في المنطق. الرابع: إن تصور قبول الأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة على الزوايا القوائم مشروط بتصور ماهية الجسم. وذلك لأنا إذا مددنا خطاً، ثم أقمنا عليه خطاً آخر، فإنه يحصل في السطح زاويتان قائمتان فقط، ويمتنع حصول الزوايا الثلاثة المتقاطعة على القوائم فيه. أما إذا فرضنا قيام خط على طرف خط آخر، فإنه تحصل فيه زاوية واحدة قائمة. ثم إذا فرضنا نزول خط آخر من نقطة التقاطع في العمق، فإنه يحدث في العمق زاويتان قائمتان. فالعقل ما لم يتصور العمق والثخن، فإنه لا يمكنه البتة تصور كيفية حصول هذه الزوايا الثلاثة المتقاطعة على القوائم. فيثبت أن تصور هذه الخاصية مشروط بسبق تصور ماهية الجسم. فلو عرفنا ماهية الجسم بهذه الخاصية لزم تعريف الشيء بما لا يعرف إلا به. وذلك باطل، ومنهي عنه في المنطق. الخامس: إن بتقدير أن يكون الحق هو أن الجسم مركب من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ، كان الطول عبارة عن تلك الأجزاء المتألفة في سمت واجد، والسطح عبارة عن تلك الأجزاء المتألفة في سمت الطول والعرض معاً، وعلى هذا التقدير فالطول والعرض والعمق ذوات قائمة بأنفسها، لا صفات قائمة بالغير. فيمتنع الحكم بكونها أموراً مقبولة القابل، ونعوتاً لذات أخرى. فيثبت بهذه الوجوه أن هذا التعريف باطل. وإنما الصواب أن يقال الجسم عبارة عن هذا الحجم، وعن هذا الثخن. ثم يقال من خواص هذه الذوات أنه يمكن أن يحصل فيها زوايا ثلاثة قائمة متقاطعة على نقطة واحدة. فجعل هذه الصفة خاصية من خواص الجسم جائز. أما جعلها معرفة لماهيته، فذلك باطل على ما قررناه
A thing is in actuality, and whatever is like that is prevented from being receptive of something else, because according to them one thing cannot be a cause for both potentiality and actuality together. And since that is the case, the recipient of the three dimensions is only the hyle (hayula). So this is what they made a definition (mu'arraf) of body (jism). It does not apply at all to body, but rather applies to the hyle of body, so it is false. The second: [The point] that a description (rasm) is [used] when the quiddity (mahiyya) is unknown in itself, so we know it by a known attribute. But the matter here is not like that. Because the body—I mean this thing that has volume (hajm) and magnitude (miqdar)—is known necessarily, and every rational person by the intuition of his intellect conceives it and recognizes it, and distinguishes between it and other existents, such as motion and rest, colors and tastes, and others. And if the conception of this quiddity is obtained in all minds and understandings, it is impossible to define it by something else. The third: The essence (dhat) of body—I mean this extended thing (mutahayyiz)—is closer to minds and intellects than its being receptive to the positing of three dimensions intersecting at right angles. For all rational people conceive the quiddity of volume and magnitude, and they do not know at all the meaning of its being receptive to three dimensions intersecting at right angles, except by precise investigation and obscure thought, and defining the manifest and clear by the obscure and hidden is prohibited in logic. The fourth: The conception of the receptivity of three dimensions intersecting at right angles is conditioned upon the prior conception of the quiddity of body. That is because if we extend a line, then erect another line upon it, only two right angles occur in the surface, and it is impossible for the three angles intersecting at right angles to occur in it. But if we suppose a line standing on the extremity of another line, then one right angle occurs in it. Then if we suppose another line descending from the point of intersection into depth, then two right angles occur in depth. So the intellect, as long as it does not conceive depth and thickness, cannot at all conceive the manner of occurrence of these three angles intersecting at right angles. Thus it is established that the conception of this property is conditioned upon prior conception of the quiddity of body. So if we defined the quiddity of body by this property, it would necessitate defining a thing by that which is only known through it. That is false and prohibited in logic. The fifth: Assuming that the truth is that body is composed of indivisible parts (ajza' allati la tatajazzau), then length would consist of those parts arranged in one direction, and surface would consist of those parts arranged in the direction of length and width together. On this assumption, length, width, and depth are self-subsistent essences, not attributes subsisting in another. So it is impossible to judge them as things received by a recipient and as attributes of another essence. Thus by these [five] ways it is established that this definition is false. The correct thing is to say: body is this volume and this thickness. Then it is said: among the properties of these essences is that three right angles intersecting at one point can occur in them. So making this attribute a property (khaassiyya) of body is permissible. But making it a definition of its quiddity is false, as we have established.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the definition of body (jism) as 'that which is receptive of three dimensions intersecting at right angles.' The author presents five arguments against this definition. The first argument: The definition confuses body with prime matter (hayula). According to the philosophers, a thing in actuality cannot be receptive of something else, because one thing cannot be both potential and actual. Since body is actual, the receptive principle is hyle, not body. So the definition applies to hyle, not body. The second argument: A definition by description (rasm) is used when the quiddity (mahiyya) is unknown. But body is known necessarily to all rational people; they conceive its volume and magnitude intuitively. Hence defining it by something else is unnecessary and impossible. The third argument: The essence of body (the extended thing) is more immediately understood than the concept of receptivity to three dimensions. The latter requires precise investigation, while the former is clear. Defining the manifest by the obscure is forbidden in logic. The fourth argument: The conception of receptivity to three dimensions presupposes the conception of body. The author gives a geometric explanation: to conceive three right angles intersecting at a point, one must first conceive depth and thickness, which are aspects of body. Therefore, defining body by that property is circular—defining a thing by something that depends on it. The fifth argument: If body is composed of indivisible parts (the atomist view), then length, width, and depth are self-subsistent entities, not attributes of another. Thus they cannot be received by a subject. Therefore, the definition fails on both the Aristotelian and atomist views. The correct approach is to define body as volume and thickness, and treat receptivity to three dimensions as a property, not a definition.

Key Terms
رسم (rasm)
ماهية (mahiyya)
جسم (jism)
بديهة (badiha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a definition of body is needed because the concept of body is abstract and unclear.
Correction: The argument shows that the concept of body is self-evident and known to everyone, so a definition by description is unnecessary.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, why is it impossible to define body by a rasm?
A) Because body has no known attributes.
B) Because the quiddity of body is unknown.
C) Because the quiddity of body is already known necessarily.
D) Because rasm is not a valid form of definition.

Key Takeaway
Body's quiddity is self-evident, so any attempted definition by description (rasm) is futile.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body is an undeniable reality you know by intuition. But do you live as if it is temporary? What changes would you make today if you truly accepted that your body is a trust from Allah?
?
The author says every rational person distinguishes body from other things by instinct. If that is so, why do you sometimes forget the purpose of your body? Write down one way you will honor your body as a gift from the Creator today.
?
You accept the existence of your body without proof. What does that tell you about the nature of certainty? Are you willing to extend that level of certainty to the unseen truths of the Quran? What holds you back?

Third Argument: Defining the Clear by the Obscure
Third argument: clear vs obscure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is logically invalid to define the clear concept of body by the obscure concept of receptivity to three dimensions.

Core Idea
The essence of body is more evident than receptivity to dimensions; defining clear by obscure is prohibited in logic.

The third argument builds on the second: not only is body known, but its essence is actually clearer than the notion of 'receptivity to three dimensions.' The latter requires deep thought, while the former is immediately grasped. This violates a logical rule against defining the manifest by the hidden.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the comparison: essence of body vs. receptivity to three dimensions.
2
Identify the logical principle
The author states that defining what is manifest (jalī) by what is obscure (khafī) is prohibited in logic. Find this statement.
This is a key rule.
3
Apply to the definition
The definition makes body's quiddity dependent on an obscure property. Ask yourself: is receptivity to three dimensions really more obscure than the concept of body?

?

?
When you study aqeedah, do you sometimes prefer intricate philosophical definitions over the simple clarity of the Quran and Sunnah? In what area of your faith are you currently at risk of defining the clear by the obscure?
?

Fourth Argument: Circularity in Defining Body
Fourth argument: circular definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that defining body by receptivity to three dimensions is circular, because conceiving that receptivity depends on prior knowledge of body.

Core Idea
You cannot define body by a property that is only understood through body itself—that's circular.

The fourth argument uses a geometric demonstration to show that the concept of 'receptivity to three right angles intersecting at a point' presupposes the concept of depth and thickness, which are aspects of body. Thus, the definition would be circular, defining body by something that depends on it.

Guided Path
1
Read the geometric explanation
Read the Arabic carefully. The author describes how we conceive angles on a line and then in depth. Follow the geometry.
This part may be technical; take your time.
2
Identify the dependence
The author argues that without prior conception of depth (body), we cannot conceive three perpendicular angles. Find where this is stated.
3
Understand the circularity
If the property depends on body, then defining body by that property is circular. Ask yourself: is this a valid logical objection?
Circular definitions are generally invalid.

الرابع: إن تصور قبول الأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة على الزوايا القوائم مشروط بتصور ماهية الجسم. وذلك لأنا إذا مددنا خطاً، ثم أقمنا عليه خطاً آخر، فإنه يحصل في السطح زاويتان قائمتان فقط، ويمتنع حصول الزوايا الثلاثة المتقاطعة على القوائم فيه. أما إذا فرضنا قيام خط على طرف خط آخر، فإنه تحصل فيه زاوية واحدة قائمة. ثم إذا فرضنا نزول خط آخر من نقطة التقاطع في العمق، فإنه يحدث في العمق زاويتان قائمتان. فالعقل ما لم يتصور العمق والثخن، فإنه لا يمكنه البتة تصور كيفية حصول هذه الزوايا الثلاثة المتقاطعة على القوائم. فيثبت أن تصور هذه الخاصية مشروط بسبق تصور ماهية الجسم. فلو عرفنا ماهية الجسم بهذه الخاصية لزم تعريف الشيء بما لا يعرف إلا به. وذلك باطل، ومنهي عنه في المنطق. الخامس: إن بتقدير أن يكون الحق هو أن الجسم مركب من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ، كان الطول عبارة عن تلك الأجزاء المتألفة في سمت واجد، والسطح عبارة عن تلك الأجزاء المتألفة في سمت الطول والعرض معاً، وعلى هذا التقدير فالطول والعرض والعمق ذوات قائمة بأنفسها، لا صفات قائمة بالغير. فيمتنع الحكم بكونها أموراً مقبولة القابل، ونعوتاً لذات أخرى. فيثبت بهذه الوجوه أن هذا التعريف باطل. وإنما الصواب أن يقال الجسم عبارة عن هذا الحجم، وعن هذا الثخن. ثم يقال من خواص هذه الذوات أنه يمكن أن يحصل فيها زوايا ثلاثة قائمة متقاطعة على نقطة واحدة. فجعل هذه الصفة خاصية من خواص الجسم جائز. أما جعلها معرفة لماهيته، فذلك باطل على ما قررناه
A thing is in actuality, and whatever is like that is prevented from being receptive of something else, because according to them one thing cannot be a cause for both potentiality and actuality together. And since that is the case, the recipient of the three dimensions is only the hyle (hayula). So this is what they made a definition (mu'arraf) of body (jism). It does not apply at all to body, but rather applies to the hyle of body, so it is false. The second: [The point] that a description (rasm) is [used] when the quiddity (mahiyya) is unknown in itself, so we know it by a known attribute. But the matter here is not like that. Because the body—I mean this thing that has volume (hajm) and magnitude (miqdar)—is known necessarily, and every rational person by the intuition of his intellect conceives it and recognizes it, and distinguishes between it and other existents, such as motion and rest, colors and tastes, and others. And if the conception of this quiddity is obtained in all minds and understandings, it is impossible to define it by something else. The third: The essence (dhat) of body—I mean this extended thing (mutahayyiz)—is closer to minds and intellects than its being receptive to the positing of three dimensions intersecting at right angles. For all rational people conceive the quiddity of volume and magnitude, and they do not know at all the meaning of its being receptive to three dimensions intersecting at right angles, except by precise investigation and obscure thought, and defining the manifest and clear by the obscure and hidden is prohibited in logic. The fourth: The conception of the receptivity of three dimensions intersecting at right angles is conditioned upon the prior conception of the quiddity of body. That is because if we extend a line, then erect another line upon it, only two right angles occur in the surface, and it is impossible for the three angles intersecting at right angles to occur in it. But if we suppose a line standing on the extremity of another line, then one right angle occurs in it. Then if we suppose another line descending from the point of intersection into depth, then two right angles occur in depth. So the intellect, as long as it does not conceive depth and thickness, cannot at all conceive the manner of occurrence of these three angles intersecting at right angles. Thus it is established that the conception of this property is conditioned upon prior conception of the quiddity of body. So if we defined the quiddity of body by this property, it would necessitate defining a thing by that which is only known through it. That is false and prohibited in logic. The fifth: Assuming that the truth is that body is composed of indivisible parts (ajza' allati la tatajazzau), then length would consist of those parts arranged in one direction, and surface would consist of those parts arranged in the direction of length and width together. On this assumption, length, width, and depth are self-subsistent essences, not attributes subsisting in another. So it is impossible to judge them as things received by a recipient and as attributes of another essence. Thus by these [five] ways it is established that this definition is false. The correct thing is to say: body is this volume and this thickness. Then it is said: among the properties of these essences is that three right angles intersecting at one point can occur in them. So making this attribute a property (khaassiyya) of body is permissible. But making it a definition of its quiddity is false, as we have established.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the definition of body (jism) as 'that which is receptive of three dimensions intersecting at right angles.' The author presents five arguments against this definition. The first argument: The definition confuses body with prime matter (hayula). According to the philosophers, a thing in actuality cannot be receptive of something else, because one thing cannot be both potential and actual. Since body is actual, the receptive principle is hyle, not body. So the definition applies to hyle, not body. The second argument: A definition by description (rasm) is used when the quiddity (mahiyya) is unknown. But body is known necessarily to all rational people; they conceive its volume and magnitude intuitively. Hence defining it by something else is unnecessary and impossible. The third argument: The essence of body (the extended thing) is more immediately understood than the concept of receptivity to three dimensions. The latter requires precise investigation, while the former is clear. Defining the manifest by the obscure is forbidden in logic. The fourth argument: The conception of receptivity to three dimensions presupposes the conception of body. The author gives a geometric explanation: to conceive three right angles intersecting at a point, one must first conceive depth and thickness, which are aspects of body. Therefore, defining body by that property is circular—defining a thing by something that depends on it. The fifth argument: If body is composed of indivisible parts (the atomist view), then length, width, and depth are self-subsistent entities, not attributes of another. Thus they cannot be received by a subject. Therefore, the definition fails on both the Aristotelian and atomist views. The correct approach is to define body as volume and thickness, and treat receptivity to three dimensions as a property, not a definition.

Key Terms
جسم (jism)
أبعاد ثلاثة (three dimensions)
زوايا قائمة (right angles)
دور (circularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the definition is not circular because dimensions are independent of body.
Correction: The geometric explanation shows that the concept of three perpendicular dimensions presupposes depth, which is an aspect of body.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth argument require to conceive the three intersecting right angles?
A) The concept of a surface.
B) The concept of depth and thickness.
C) The concept of a line.
D) The concept of a point.

Key Takeaway
Defining body by receptivity to three perpendicular dimensions is circular because that receptivity is only understood through body itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you have any belief about your own identity that is actually defined by something that only exists through you? What would happen if you had to define yourself without that crutch?
?
The author warns that defining body by a property dependent on body is invalid. How often do you define your relationship with Allah by worldly outcomes that themselves depend on your existence? How can you break that circle?
?
If circular reasoning is false in logic, what areas of your faith might be vulnerable to it? Are you willing to examine your assumptions about Allah’s attributes to ensure they are not defined by created things?

Fifth Argument: Atomist View and Conclusion
Fifth argument: atomist view and resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even on the atomist view, the definition fails because dimensions become self-subsistent, not received, and you will learn the correct way to speak about body and its properties.

Core Idea
Even if body is composed of atoms, dimensions are self-subsistent, not received; the definition fails universally.

The fifth and final argument considers an alternative metaphysical framework: the atomist view that body is composed of indivisible parts. Even then, length, width, and depth would be self-subsistent entities, not attributes received by a subject. Hence the definition fails. The author concludes by stating the correct approach: define body as volume/thickness and treat receptivity as a property.

Guided Path
1
Read the atomist scenario
Read the passage that presents the assumption of indivisible parts. Understand how length, width, depth would be conceptualized.
2
Understand the conclusion
The author argues that on this view, dimensions are self-subsistent (dhawat qa'ima bi-anfusiha), so they cannot be received. Why does that invalidate the definition?
3
Learn the correct alternative
The author proposes that we should say 'body is this volume and thickness' and then attribute the three angles as a property (khaassiyya), not a definition.

يكون الشيء بالفعل وما كان كذلك امتنع كونه قابلاً لشيء آخر، لأن عندهم الشيء الواحد لا يكون سبباً للقوة وللفعل معا. ولما كان ذلك كذلك، كان القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة ليس إلا الهيولى فهذا الذي جعلوه معرفاً للجسم. لم يصدق البتة على الجسم، وإنما صدق على هيولى الجسم، فكان باطلاً. الثاني: إن الرسم عبارة عما إذا كانت الماهية مجهولة في نفسها، فنعرفها بصفة معلومة. وليس الأمر ههنا كذلك. لأن الجسم أعني هذا الشيء الذي له حجم ومقدار أمر معلوم بالضرورة، وكل عاقل فإنه ببديهة عقله يتصوره ويتعرفه، ويميز بينه وبين سائر الموجودات. مثل الحركة والسكون، والألوان والطعوم، وغيرها. وإذا كان تصور هذه الماهية حاصلاً في جمع العقول والأفهام، امتنع تعريفها بشيء آخر. الثالث: إن ذات الجسم أعني هذا الشيء المتحيز أقرب إلى الأفهام والعقول من كونه قابلاً لفرض الأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة على الزوايا القوائم. فإن جميع العقلاء يتصورون ماهية الحجم والمقدار، ولا يعرفون البتة معنى كونه قابلاً للأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة على الزوايا القوائم، إلا بتدقيق النظر، وغامض الفكر، وتعريف الظاهر الجلي، بالغامض الخفي منهي عنه في المنطق. Deriving the Four Views on Body Composition
Author derives four views from two dichotomies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun derived four possible views on the composition of bodies using two dichotomies.

Core Idea
Two dichotomies yield four views.

Before examining specific doctrines, the author establishes a logical framework. By considering whether a body's division exists in actuality or not, and whether it is finite or infinite, he shows that only four positions are logically possible. This lesson traces that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial division
Read the passage's opening dichotomy: body is either simple (homogeneous) or composite (made of parts). This sets the stage for the logical derivation.
Simple and composite are defined in terms of potential divisibility.
2
Apply the two dichotomies
Notice how the author then asks: is division actual or potential? And is it finite or infinite? These two pairs generate four combinations.
The phrase 'فهذا التقسيم' introduces the binary choices.
3
List the four views
The four categories are: (1) composite with finite parts (atomism), (2) composite with infinite parts, (3) simple with finite potential division, (4) simple with infinite potential division. Each view corresponds to a known school.
The author only elaborates the first view in this chapter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في شرح مذاهب أصل العالم في الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ

اعلم أن الجسم إما أن يكون بسيطاً وإما أن يكون مركباً. أما المركب فلا شك أنه مركب من أجزاء متناهية موجودة بالفعل. وأما البسيط فلا شك أنه قابل للقسمة الوهمية. فنقول: هذه القسمة الممكنة إما أن تكون موجودة بالفعل، وإما أن لا تكون. وعلى التقديرين فتلك القسمة إما أن تكون متناهية، أو غير متناهية.

فخرج بسبب هذين النوعين من التقسيم: أقسام أربعة لا مزيد عليها

الأول: أن يقال: الأجسام مركبة تركيباً بالفعل من أجزاء متناهية. وهذا مذهب جمهور المتكلمين. وزعموا: أن كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء لا يقبل القسمة لا كسراً ولا قطعاً ولا وهماً ولا فرضاً. والفرق بين هذه الاعتبارات الأربعة: أن نقول: أسهل وجوه القسمة: هو الكسر. مثل: انكسار الخزف والحجر، ثم يليه في الرتبة: القطع. مثل: القطعة من الذهب والحديد، فإنها لا تنكسر، إلا أنه يمكن قطعها بالآلات القطاعة. ثم يليه في المرتبة الثالثة: الوهم، لأن الشيء قد لا يكون قابلاً للقسمة الانفكاكية، مثل: الفلك. فإنه عند الفلاسفة لا يقبل الخرق والتمزق، إلا أنه قابل للقسمة الوهمية. ثم يليه في المرتبة الرابعة: الفرض. وهو الجزء الذي يبلغ في الصغر إلى حيث يعجز الوهم عن تخيله وتصوره. وإذا كان كذلك، امتنع حصول القسمة الوهمية فيه لأن الشيء الذي لا يصل الوهم والخيال إلى تصوره، فإنه يمتنع حصول القسمة الوهمية فيه إلا أنه يكون قابلاً للقسمة الفرضية. فإن الوهم والخيال، وإن عجزا عن إدراكه وتصوره، إلا أنه في نفسه موصوف بكونه بحيث يتميز أحد جانبيه عن الجانب الثاني.

فهذا هو بيان الفرق بين هذه الوجوه الأربعة. وهذا هو شرح مذهب القائلين بكون الجسم مركباً من أجزاء متناهية، كل واحد منها لا يقبل القسمة البتة.
The third chapter on the explanation of the views of the origin of the world concerning the indivisible part. Know that the body is either simple or composite. As for the composite, there is no doubt that it is composed of finite parts existing in actuality. And as for the simple, there is no doubt that it is divisible in imagination. We say: this possible division either exists in actuality or not. In both cases, that division is either finite or infinite. So due to these two types of division, four categories emerge without addition. The first: that it is said: bodies are composite with an actual composition from finite parts. This is the doctrine of the majority of the mutakallimun. They claimed that each one of those parts does not accept division, neither by breaking, nor by cutting, nor by imagination, nor by supposition. And the difference between these four considerations: we say: the easiest way of division is breaking, such as the breaking of pottery and stone; then next in rank is cutting, such as a piece of gold and iron, for it does not break, but it can be cut with cutting tools; then next in the third rank is imagination, because a thing may not be susceptible to separative division, such as the celestial sphere, for according to the philosophers it does not accept tearing and rending, but it is divisible in imagination; then next in the fourth rank is supposition, which is the part that reaches in smallness to the extent that imagination is unable to conceive it and picture it. And if that is so, the occurrence of imaginative division in it is impossible, because the thing which imagination and fancy do not reach to conceive, the occurrence of imaginative division in it is impossible, except that it is susceptible to suppositional division. For imagination and fancy, even if they are incapable of grasping it and conceiving it, it is nevertheless in itself described as being such that one of its sides is distinguished from the other side. So this is the explanation of the difference between these four modes. And this is the explanation of the doctrine of those who hold that the body is composed of finite parts, each one of which does not accept division at all.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage defines the problem of the composition of bodies and explains the first view (atomism). The author begins by dividing bodies into simple and composite. He then discusses possible divisions (actual/potential, finite/infinite) and derives four possible views. He then elaborates on the first view, the position of the majority of the mutakallimun, that bodies are composed of atoms that are indivisible. To clarify 'indivisible', he explains four degrees of division: breaking (kusr), cutting (qat'), imagination (wahm), and supposition (fard). Breaking is the easiest, cutting requires tools, imagination is not physical, and supposition is applied to extremely small parts. He concludes that the atom is indivisible in all these ways, confirming the mutakallimun's stance.

Atomism and the Four Types of Division
Author explains first view (atomism) and defines four types of division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's claim that atoms are indivisible and the four types of division used to define that indivisibility.

Core Idea
The atom resists breaking, cutting, imagination, and supposition.

The first view—that bodies are composed of finite, indivisible parts—is the position of the majority of mutakallimun. To clarify what 'indivisible' means, they distinguish four degrees of division, from the physical (breaking, cutting) to the conceptual (imagination, supposition). This lesson explains each type and hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doctrine
Read the passage's opening statement: bodies are actually composed of finite parts, each indivisible. This is the atomist view.
The term 'indivisible part' is key.
2
Learn the four types of division
The text lists four methods: breaking, cutting, imagination, and supposition. Note their order from easiest to most subtle.
Breaking is physical; supposition is mental and most refined.
3
Understand the hierarchy
Each type is defined with examples. Breaking applies to brittle things, cutting to metals, imagination to celestial spheres, supposition to extremely tiny parts. The atom is indivisible in all four ways.
Suppositional division is for parts so small that imagination fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في شرح مذاهب أصل العالم في الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ

اعلم أن الجسم إما أن يكون بسيطاً وإما أن يكون مركباً. أما المركب فلا شك أنه مركب من أجزاء متناهية موجودة بالفعل. وأما البسيط فلا شك أنه قابل للقسمة الوهمية. فنقول: هذه القسمة الممكنة إما أن تكون موجودة بالفعل، وإما أن لا تكون. وعلى التقديرين فتلك القسمة إما أن تكون متناهية، أو غير متناهية.

فخرج بسبب هذين النوعين من التقسيم: أقسام أربعة لا مزيد عليها

الأول: أن يقال: الأجسام مركبة تركيباً بالفعل من أجزاء متناهية. وهذا مذهب جمهور المتكلمين. وزعموا: أن كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء لا يقبل القسمة لا كسراً ولا قطعاً ولا وهماً ولا فرضاً. والفرق بين هذه الاعتبارات الأربعة: أن نقول: أسهل وجوه القسمة: هو الكسر. مثل: انكسار الخزف والحجر، ثم يليه في الرتبة: القطع. مثل: القطعة من الذهب والحديد، فإنها لا تنكسر، إلا أنه يمكن قطعها بالآلات القطاعة. ثم يليه في المرتبة الثالثة: الوهم، لأن الشيء قد لا يكون قابلاً للقسمة الانفكاكية، مثل: الفلك. فإنه عند الفلاسفة لا يقبل الخرق والتمزق، إلا أنه قابل للقسمة الوهمية. ثم يليه في المرتبة الرابعة: الفرض. وهو الجزء الذي يبلغ في الصغر إلى حيث يعجز الوهم عن تخيله وتصوره. وإذا كان كذلك، امتنع حصول القسمة الوهمية فيه لأن الشيء الذي لا يصل الوهم والخيال إلى تصوره، فإنه يمتنع حصول القسمة الوهمية فيه إلا أنه يكون قابلاً للقسمة الفرضية. فإن الوهم والخيال، وإن عجزا عن إدراكه وتصوره، إلا أنه في نفسه موصوف بكونه بحيث يتميز أحد جانبيه عن الجانب الثاني.

فهذا هو بيان الفرق بين هذه الوجوه الأربعة. وهذا هو شرح مذهب القائلين بكون الجسم مركباً من أجزاء متناهية، كل واحد منها لا يقبل القسمة البتة.
The third chapter on the explanation of the views of the origin of the world concerning the indivisible part. Know that the body is either simple or composite. As for the composite, there is no doubt that it is composed of finite parts existing in actuality. And as for the simple, there is no doubt that it is divisible in imagination. We say: this possible division either exists in actuality or not. In both cases, that division is either finite or infinite. So due to these two types of division, four categories emerge without addition. The first: that it is said: bodies are composite with an actual composition from finite parts. This is the doctrine of the majority of the mutakallimun. They claimed that each one of those parts does not accept division, neither by breaking, nor by cutting, nor by imagination, nor by supposition. And the difference between these four considerations: we say: the easiest way of division is breaking, such as the breaking of pottery and stone; then next in rank is cutting, such as a piece of gold and iron, for it does not break, but it can be cut with cutting tools; then next in the third rank is imagination, because a thing may not be susceptible to separative division, such as the celestial sphere, for according to the philosophers it does not accept tearing and rending, but it is divisible in imagination; then next in the fourth rank is supposition, which is the part that reaches in smallness to the extent that imagination is unable to conceive it and picture it. And if that is so, the occurrence of imaginative division in it is impossible, because the thing which imagination and fancy do not reach to conceive, the occurrence of imaginative division in it is impossible, except that it is susceptible to suppositional division. For imagination and fancy, even if they are incapable of grasping it and conceiving it, it is nevertheless in itself described as being such that one of its sides is distinguished from the other side. So this is the explanation of the difference between these four modes. And this is the explanation of the doctrine of those who hold that the body is composed of finite parts, each one of which does not accept division at all.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage defines the problem of the composition of bodies and explains the first view (atomism). The author begins by dividing bodies into simple and composite. He then discusses possible divisions (actual/potential, finite/infinite) and derives four possible views. He then elaborates on the first view, the position of the majority of the mutakallimun, that bodies are composed of atoms that are indivisible. To clarify 'indivisible', he explains four degrees of division: breaking (kusr), cutting (qat'), imagination (wahm), and supposition (fard). Breaking is the easiest, cutting requires tools, imagination is not physical, and supposition is applied to extremely small parts. He concludes that the atom is indivisible in all these ways, confirming the mutakallimun's stance.

Correction: The text explicitly states that the atom does not accept division by breaking, cutting, imagination, or supposition. It is indivisible in every possible way.

?
The atom is so small that imagination cannot perceive it, but it still has distinction. What subtle aspects of your spiritual life—like hidden sincerity or unnoticed sins—might be 'unimaginable' to you but fully known to Allah? How will you increase your awareness of them?
