The Three Atomistic Claims: An Overview
Classification of three atomistic claims and the foundational premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three claims that the author aims to prove: that motion, time, and body are composed of indivisible parts.

Core Idea
Atomism in kalam rests on three interrelated claims about motion, time, and body.

The author begins by stating his overall plan: to demonstrate that motion, time, and body are all composed of indivisible units. This sets the stage for the detailed arguments that follow. Before diving into the proofs, it is essential to grasp the threefold structure of the project.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening premise
Read the author's statement that motion must be composed of successive occurrences, and that time is composed of successive instants, leading to the composition of body. This is the foundational premise.
2
Identify the three types
The author lists three demands (مطالب) of the chapter. Find each one and note what it claims.
The three are: proof about motion, proof about time, proof about body.
3
Understand the interdependence
Notice that the third claim depends on the first two. The composition of body is argued from the composition of motion and time.
This is a chain of reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتتالية. وأما نحن فإنا نقيم الدلالة على أنه يجب أن تكون الحركة مركبة من حصولات متعاقبة في أحياز متلاصقة، وعلى أن الزمان مركب من آنات متتالية متعاقبة. ثم نفرع عليه: أن الجسم يجب أن يكون مركباً من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ. فلتكن هذه المقدمة معلومة.

ثم نقول: مطالب هذا الفصل محصورة في ثلاثة أنواع:
أحدها: تقرير الدلائل على أن الحركة مركبة من حصولات متعاقبة بحيث يكون كل واحد منها غير قابل للقسمة أصلاً،
وثانيها: تقرير الدلائل على أن الزمان مركب من آنات متتالية متلاصقة، بحيث لا يكون شيء منها قابلاً للقسمة أصلاً.
وثالثها: تقرير أنه متى كان الحال في الحركة وفي الزمان على ما ذكرناه، فإنه يجب كون الجسم مؤلفاً من أجزاء، كل واحد منها لا يقبل القسمة أصلاً.

أما النوع الأول: وهو ذكر الدليل على أن الحركة عبارة عن حصولات متعاقبة، في أحياز متلاصقة، بحيث يكون كل واحد منها غير قابل للقسمة. فنقول: الذي يدل على صحة ما قلنا وجوه:
الحجة الأولى: أن نقول: الجسم لم يكن متحركاً، ثم صار متحركاً. فالحركة أمر موجود في نفسها. إذ لو لم يحدث في الجسم حال كونه متحركاً أمر من الأمور، لزم أن يقال: إنه في نفسه بعد الحركة، كما كان قبل الحركة. لكنه قبل الحركة غير متحرك، فهو بعد الحركة غير متحرك. فالمتحرك غير متحرك. هذا خلف. فثبت: أن الحركة أمر موجود.

ثم نقول: لا يخلو إما أن يحصل منها شيء في الحال، أو لم يحصل. فإن لم يحصل منها شيء في الحال، استحال أن يكون ماضياً أو مستقبلاً. لأن الماضي هو الذي كان موجوداً في زمان كان حاضراً، والمستقبل هو الذي يتوقع حضوره في زمان سيحضر. فلو امتنع أن يوجد في الحاضر شيء من أجزاء الحركة، لامتنع أن يصير ماضياً، أو يكون مستقبلاً. وعلى هذا التقدير، فإنه لا يوجد شيء من أجزاء الحركة، ولا من مجموعه. لا في الماضي ولا في المستقبل ولا في الحال. ولو كان الأمر كذلك، لوجب أن لا توجد الحركة أصلاً. وذلك باطل على ما بينا. ولما بطل هذا القسم، ثبت أنه لا بد وأن يوجد في الحال الحاضر شيء من أجزاء الحركة.

ثم نقول: ذلك الحاضر، إما أن يمكن فرض جزأين فيه، بحيث يكون أحدهما قبل الآخر، أو لا يمكن. والأول باطل. وإلا لكان عند حصول النصف الأول منه لا يكون النصف الثاني منه موجوداً، وعند مجيء النصف الثاني منه يكون النصف الأول منه فائناً منقرضاً. وحينئذ لا يكون الحاصل حاصلاً بل يكون الحاصل أحد نصفيه فقط. ثم إنا نعيد التقسيم المذكور في ذلك النصف. فإن كان هو أيضاً منقسماً، فحينئذ لا يكون هو أيضاً موجوداً. والحاصل: أن كل ما كان منقسماً إلى قسمين، يكون أحد نصفيه سابقاً على الآخر، فإنه يمتنع أن يكون بتمامه موجوداً. وهذا ينعكس انعكاس النقيض: إن ما يكون بتمامه موجوداً، فإنه لا يكون قابلاً للقسمة المذكورة. فثبت: أن الجزء الحاضر من الحركة، غير قابل للقسمة. ولا شك أن عند انقضاء هذا الجزء الحاضر، لا بد وأن يحصل شيء آخر.
The successive. And as for us, we establish the proof that motion must be composed of successive occurrences in adjacent positions, and that time is composed of successive consecutive instants. Then we derive from it: that the body must be composed of parts that are indivisible. So let this premise be known.

Then we say: The demands of this chapter are confined to three types:
One: Establishing the proofs that motion is composed of successive occurrences such that each one of them is indivisible essentially,
Second: Establishing the proofs that time is composed of successive adjacent instants, such that none of them is divisible essentially,
Third: Establishing that when the case regarding motion and time is as we have mentioned, then it is necessary that the body be composed of parts, each one of which does not accept division essentially.

As for the first type: which is the mention of the proof that motion is [composed] of successive occurrences in adjacent positions, such that each one of them is indivisible. We say: That which indicates the correctness of what we have said are [several] aspects:
The first proof: That we say: The body was not moving, then it became moving. So motion is an existent matter in itself. For if no thing among things occurred in the body while it was moving, it would necessarily be said that it is in itself after motion as it was before motion. But before motion it was not moving, so after motion it is not moving. So the moving thing is not moving. This is a contradiction [khalf]. So it is established: that motion is an existent matter.

Then we say: It is not free from either that something of it occurs in the present moment, or it does not occur. If nothing of it occurs in the present moment, it is impossible for it to be past or future. Because the past is that which existed in a time that was present, and the future is that whose presence is expected in a time that will be present. So if it is prevented that some parts of motion exist in the present, it would be prevented from becoming past or being future. On this estimation, nothing of the parts of motion would exist, nor of its totality. Neither in the past, nor in the future, nor in the present. And if it were so, it would necessarily follow that motion does not exist at all. That is false as we have shown. And when this division is invalidated, it is established that there must necessarily exist in the present moment something of the parts of motion.

Then we say: That present [part], either it is possible to posit two parts in it such that one is before the other, or [it is] not possible. The first is false. Otherwise, at the occurrence of the first half of it, the second half of it would not exist, and at the coming of the second half of it, the first half of it would be perished and extinct. And then the [thing] obtained would not be obtained, but rather the obtained would be only one of its two halves. Then we repeat the aforementioned division in that half. If it too is divisible, then it too would not exist. And the result: that everything that is divisible into two halves, one of its halves being prior to the other, it is impossible for it to exist in its entirety. And this converts by the contrapositive: that whatever exists in its entirety, it is not susceptible to the mentioned division. So it is established: that the present part of motion is indivisible. And there is no doubt that at the expiration of this present part, another thing must necessarily occur.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a classification and a proof. The author first states the overall plan of the chapter: to establish three claims about atomism — that motion is composed of indivisible occurrences, that time is composed of indivisible instants, and that body is composed of indivisible parts. Then he begins the first claim with an initial proof (al-hujja al-ula).

The proof proceeds in stages:
1. Motion exists as a real attribute: If a body becomes moving after not being moving, then something new must have occurred in it, otherwise it would still be non-moving (a contradiction). So motion is an existent thing.
2. Something of motion must exist in the present moment: If nothing of motion existed in the present, it could not be past or future, and so motion would not exist at all, which contradicts its established existence. Therefore, a part of motion exists now.
3. That present part must be indivisible: If it were divisible into two parts with one before the other, then at the time of the first half, the second half would not exist, and at the time of the second half, the first would be gone; thus the whole could never exist simultaneously. Hence anything that exists in its entirety cannot have such division. By contrapositive, the present part of motion, which exists entirely now, must be indivisible. Consequently, motion consists of a succession of such indivisible present parts.

Key Terms
حركة (motion)
زمان (time)
جسم (body)
جزء لا يتجزأ (indivisible part)
حصولات متعاقبة (successive occurrences)
آنات متتالية (successive instants)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the three main topics (مطالب) of this chapter?
A) Proof about motion, proof about time, proof about body.
B) Proof about motion, proof about speed, proof about distance.
C) Proof about time, proof about space, proof about substance.
D) Proof about atoms, proof about void, proof about motion.

Key Takeaway
The chapter aims to prove that motion, time, and body are all composed of indivisible units, with the proof for body resting on the proofs for motion and time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if each moment of your day is a separate instant under Allah's direct creation—or do you assume a continuous flow that lulls you into heedlessness? How would your accountability change if you believed every 'now' is a fresh gift from Him?
?
If your body is composed of indivisible parts, each sustained by Allah at every instant, then what part of your physical existence can you claim as your own? How does that humbling thought reshape your gratitude and reliance on Him?
?
When you consider that even motion—your walking, your gestures—is a series of discrete creations, does it not press you to ask: 'Am I moving in obedience or disobedience in this very step?' How will you remind yourself of this before your next action?

The First Proof: Motion Exists and Its Present Part Is Indivisible
The first proof: motion is composed of indivisible present occurrences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three-step argument that motion is real, that a part of it exists now, and that this part must be indivisible.

Core Idea
Motion must consist of indivisible present parts, otherwise it could not exist as a whole.

Having outlined the three claims, the author now presents the first proof for the composition of motion. The argument proceeds dialectically: first establishing that motion is a real existent, then showing that something of it must exist in the present moment, and finally demonstrating that this present part cannot be divided. This core argument underlies the atomistic view of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire proof once
Read the Arabic passage from 'أما النوع الأول' to the end. Then read the translation to get the overall flow.
The argument is dense; do not worry about details yet.
2
Follow the first sub-argument
The author argues that motion exists because if a body becomes moving, something new must occur; otherwise it would still be non-moving. This is a contradiction. Identify this step.
This establishes motion as an existent attribute.
3
Follow the second sub-argument
The author then argues that some part of motion must exist in the present. Otherwise, motion could not be past or future, and would not exist at all. Find this step.
4
Follow the third sub-argument
The present part of motion must be indivisible. If it were divisible into two halves with one before the other, the whole could never exist simultaneously. Therefore, by contrapositive, what exists entirely now must be indivisible.
This is the key conclusion.

المتتالية. وأما نحن فإنا نقيم الدلالة على أنه يجب أن تكون الحركة مركبة من حصولات متعاقبة في أحياز متلاصقة، وعلى أن الزمان مركب من آنات متتالية متعاقبة. ثم نفرع عليه: أن الجسم يجب أن يكون مركباً من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ. فلتكن هذه المقدمة معلومة.

ثم نقول: مطالب هذا الفصل محصورة في ثلاثة أنواع:
أحدها: تقرير الدلائل على أن الحركة مركبة من حصولات متعاقبة بحيث يكون كل واحد منها غير قابل للقسمة أصلاً،

The successive. And as for us, we establish the proof that motion must be composed of successive occurrences in adjacent positions, and that time is composed of successive consecutive instants. Then we derive from it: that the body must be composed of parts that are indivisible. So let this premise be known.

Then we say: It is not free from either that something of it occurs in the present moment, or it does not occur. Commentary
This passage is a combination of a classification and a proof. The author first states the overall plan of the chapter: to establish three claims about atomism — that motion is composed of indivisible occurrences, that time is composed of indivisible instants, and that body is composed of indivisible parts. Then he begins the first claim with an initial proof (al-hujja al-ula).

The proof proceeds in stages:
1. Motion exists as a real attribute: If a body becomes moving after not being moving, then something new must have occurred in it, otherwise it would still be non-moving (a contradiction). So motion is an existent thing.
2. Something of motion must exist in the present moment: If nothing of motion existed in the present, it could not be past or future, and so motion would not exist at all, which contradicts its established existence. Therefore, a part of motion exists now.
3. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is a continuous, infinitely divisible flow.
Correction: The author argues that motion must be composed of discrete indivisible occurrences, as otherwise it could not exist as a whole in the present.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the present part of motion?
A) It is divisible into two halves that exist simultaneously.
B) It is indivisible, because if divisible it could not exist entirely at once.
C) It is infinitely divisible like a continuum.
D) It does not exist; only the past and future parts exist.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that motion consists of a succession of indivisible present occurrences, with each part existing entirely at its moment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your every action in this indivisible present moment is an unbroken whole before Allah. What are you doing right now that cannot be undone? Will you fill it with obedience or waste it?
?
The proof shows that without present parts, motion would not exist. Your life is a succession of such moments. Which moment are you truly living for—the past that is gone, the future that may not come, or this present instant that is slipping away?
?
Before motion began, the body was not moving. What has changed in you after encountering this proof? Has it awakened you to the Creator who holds every atom of your existence moment by moment?

First Sophistical Objection: The 'Now' Analogy
Fakhr al-Razi presents the first sophistical objection regarding the necessity of a present part of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first sophistical objection Fakhr al-Razi raises against the proof for motion, and how it uses the analogy of the 'now' to deny the need for a present part of motion.

Core Idea
The 'now' itself is never present—so why must a part of motion be present now?

In this passage, Fakhr al-Razi introduces a sophisticated objection that targets a key premise in the standard proof for motion. He compares the required 'present part of motion' to the elusive nature of the 'now', arguing that just as the 'now' is never actually present without infinite regress, the same may hold for a part of motion. This objection is framed as a challenge to self-evident truths, characteristic of sophistical interrogation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the author's reference to a separate treatise and the sophistical nature.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the objection's structure
The objection starts with 'We do not grant' and then gives a reason. Find the premise being denied and the analogy used.
3
Analyze the 'now' analogy
Understand how the now becomes past and future without being present. Why would that imply an infinite regress if it were present?
Think about temporal moments.
4
Reflect on its sophistical character
Consider why the author calls these questions 'sophistical.' They cast doubt on self-evident truths. Is this a valid challenge or a fallacy?
The author will later answer this objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم: أنا كنا قد كتبنا رسالة مفردة في مسألة الجوهر الفرد، وأوردنا على هذا الدليل: أسئلة جارية مجرى سؤالات السوفسطائية، في التشكيك في البديهيات. فمن أراد تلك الأسئلة فعليه بذلك الكتاب إلا أنا نورد ههنا من تلك السؤالات، ما يختص بهذا الدليل. فنقول: [السؤال الأول]: لا نسلم أنه لا بد وأن يحصل في الحال شيء من أجزاء الحركة. قوله: (لو لم يحصل منها شيء في الحال، لامتنع أن يصير ماضياً ومستقبلاً)، قلنا: لا نسلم. فإن الآن يصير ماضياً ومستقبلاً، مع أنه لا يصدق على الآن: أنه حاضر في الآن. وإلا لزم التسلسل.
Know: we have already composed a separate treatise on the question of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), and we raised against this proof questions that run in the manner of sophistical interrogations, in casting doubt on self-evident truths. So whoever desires those questions should consult that book. However, we present here from those questions what specifically pertains to this proof. We say: [First question]: We do not grant that some part of the motion must occur in the present moment. His statement: 'If nothing of it occurs in the present moment, it would be impossible for it to become past and future.' We say: we do not grant that. For the 'now' (al-ān) becomes past and future, despite the fact that 'now' is not truly present in the now; otherwise, an infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage introduces the author's treatment of objections. He mentions he has written a separate work on the indivisible atom and that the objections raised there are sophistical, casting doubt on self-evident truths. Here he presents only objections specific to this proof. The first objection denies the premise that a part of motion must exist now. The author (or the objector) argues that the 'now' itself becomes past and future without being present in the now, otherwise there would be an infinite regress of 'now's. So the necessity of a present part is not granted. This is a classic sophistical challenge to the proof.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد
سوفسطائية
بديهيات
الآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection is the author's own view and refutes the proof.
Correction: The author is presenting a sophistical challenge that he intends to answer; it is not his final position.

Checkpoint
In the first objection, what analogy is used to deny the need for a present part of motion?
A) The indivisible atom
B) The 'now' becoming past and future without being present
C) The infinite regress of motion
D) The impossibility of motion

Key Takeaway
The first sophistical objection uses the analogy of the 'now' to argue that a present part of motion is not necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows how a clever analogy can make you doubt something obvious. When was the last time you used a clever argument to avoid accepting a clear truth about yourself—and what was the real reason you resisted it?
?
The sophistical objection challenges even self-evident truths. Is there a truth about your relationship with Allah that you have been avoiding because it feels uncomfortable or demands change? Will you face it today or keep hiding behind reasons?
?
The 'now' is never present—it always slips away. What have you been postponing, telling yourself 'not now,' knowing deep down that the perfect moment never arrives? What step can you take in this very moment that you have been putting off?

Second Objection: The Limit of Motion
Second objection: what occurs in the now is the limit of motion, not motion itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the kalām argument that motion consists of consecutive indivisible parts, and why it does not force that conclusion.

Core Idea
The limit of motion is indivisible, but divisible parts may intervene.

This lesson presents the second objection to the kalām proof that motion requires consecutive indivisible parts. The objection concedes that something of motion occurs in the now, but argues that it is only the limit of motion—an indivisible point—not motion itself. The objector then challenges the claim that this forces a succession of indivisibles, suggesting divisible parts could separate them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the concession: 'We grant that something must occur in the now.'
2
Identify the key claim
What does the objector say occurs in the now? He says it is the limit of motion, not motion itself. The limit is indivisible.
The term 'limit of motion' (طرف الحركة) is crucial.
3
Understand the counterargument and reply
The objector anticipates a response: 'When that limit perishes, another indivisible must occur.' But he replies: why cannot a divisible thing occur between them?
This blocks the conclusion that indivisibles follow each other directly.
4
See the consequence for the proof
The objection shows that the proof does not force a succession of indivisibles. The existence of a divisible interval is possible.
5
Reflect on the lesson's insight
Consider how this objection weakens the original argument for atomism in motion. It does not deny indivisible parts, but their direct succession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني: سلمنا: أنه لا بد وأن يحصل في الآن الحاضر، شيء من الحركة. إلا أن الحاصل في الآن، هو طرف الحركة، لا نفس الحركة. و طرف الحركة عندنا شيء غير منقسم. لا يقال: فعند فناء ذلك الشيء الذي سميتموه بطرف الحركة، لا بد وأن يحدث شيء آخر غير منقسم. وحينئذ يحصل المطلوب، لأنها نقول: لم لا يجوز أن يحصل بينهما شيء منقسم؟ وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه لا يلزم تعاقب الأشياء التي لا تتجزأ.

And the limit of motion, according to us, is an indivisible thing. It cannot be said: 'When that thing which you call the limit of motion perishes, another indivisible thing must occur, and thus the desired conclusion is obtained.' Because we say: why is it not possible that a divisible thing occurs between them? On this assumption, the succession of indivisible things does not follow.

Commentary
The second objection concedes the necessity of something present in the now, but identifies it as the limit of motion (طرف الحركة) rather than motion itself. This limit is indivisible. The objector then anticipates a response: 'When that limit perishes, another indivisible thing must occur, leading to a succession of indivisibles.' But he rejects that, arguing that a divisible thing could occur between them. Thus the proof's conclusion that motion consists of consecutive indivisibles is not forced.

Third Objection: Divisibility of the Present Part
The third objection questions the indivisibility of the present part and proposes simultaneous occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection to the argument for the indivisibility of the present part of motion, and how the objector challenges the proof by proposing simultaneous occurrence of parts.

Core Idea
The objection suggests that parts could occur all at once, thus challenging the proof of indivisibility.

In this lesson, we examine the third objection presented by the opponent. The objector grants that something of motion occurs in the present moment, but questions why that thing cannot be divisible. The key move is to propose that the two halves of the divisible part could occur simultaneously (دفعة واحدة), thus preserving the existence of the whole in the present.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the structure: the objector grants a premise, then poses a question, then quotes the original argument's reply, and finally offers a counter-response.

2

The objector concedes that motion occurs in the present (سلمنا) but questions the necessity of indivisibility. He attacks the assumption that divisibility implies temporal priority.
3

The objector proposes that the hypothetical halves could occur simultaneously. Think about how this undermines the original argument that if divisible, the halves would be sequential and the whole wouldn't exist in the present.
This is a direct challenge to the proof's premise.
4
Reflect on the debate
Consider how the original argument might respond to this objection. Does the proposal of simultaneous halves actually refute the proof, or does it require further assumptions?
This leads to deeper discussion about the nature of time and motion.

The Present Defined: Resolving the First Objection
First objection denies necessity of a present part of motion; answer defines 'the present'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defines 'the present' to resolve the objection about motion and avoid infinite regress.

Core Idea
The present is what exists and is neither past nor future — no container needed.

This passage presents the first objection to the argument about motion and time. The objector claims that a part of motion need not occur in the present moment, using the example of the 'now' itself. The author answers by clarifying what 'present' truly means, thereby dissolving the objection without falling into infinite regress. Understanding this answer sharpens our grasp of temporal concepts in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and opponent's argument
Start by reading the objection: the opponent denies that a part of motion must occur in the present. Note the reasoning: if nothing occurred now, motion couldn't become past or future.
This objection challenges a key premise about motion's continuity.
2
Examine the objector's counter-example about 'now'
The objector points out that the 'now' itself becomes past and future, yet it is not 'present in the now' without regress. Understand why this is posed as a problem.
The objector argues that defining 'present' as containing part of motion leads to infinite regress.
3
Study the author's definition and its effect
The author responds by defining 'the present' as that which exists and is neither past nor future. The 'now' fits this definition directly, so no infinite regress follows. See how this answer dissolves the objection.
The key is that 'present' does not mean being contained in something else.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الأول : لا نسلم أنه لا بد وأن يحصل في الحال شيء من أجزاء الحركة. قوله : ( لو لم يحصل منها شيء في الحال ، لامتنع أن يصير ماضياً ومستقبلاً )، قلنا : لا نسلم . فإن الآن يصير ماضياً ومستقبلاً ، مع أنه لا يصدق على الآن : أنه حاضر في الآن . وإلا لزم التسلسل . والجواب عن السؤال الأول : أن نقول : التفرقة بين الماضي وبين المستقبل وبين الحال : معلوم بالضرورة . فنحن نعني بالحاضر : الموجود الذي لا يكون ماضياً ولا مستقبلاً . ولا شك أن الآن الحاضر كذلك ، فيكون الآن حاضراً ولا نعني بالحاضر أن يكون مظروفاً في شيء ، وأن يكون حاصلاً في شيء آخر ، حتى يلزمنا التسلسل .
First objection: We do not accept that necessarily something of the parts of motion occurs in the present moment. He said: 'If nothing of it occurred in the present moment, it would be impossible for it to become past and future.' We say: We do not accept. For the 'now' becomes past and future, yet it is not true of the 'now' that it is present in the 'now'. Otherwise an infinite regress would follow. The answer to the first objection: We say: The distinction between past, future, and present is known by necessity. We mean by 'the present': that which exists and is neither past nor future. Without doubt the present 'now' is like that, so the 'now' is present, and we do not mean by 'the present' that it is contained in something or that it occurs in something else, such that an infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage contains the first objection and its answer. The objector denies that a part of motion must occur in the present moment. The opponent's argument is that if nothing occurred now, motion could never become past or future. The objector rejects this, pointing out that the 'now' itself becomes past and future, yet the 'now' is not present in the 'now'—otherwise an infinite regress would ensue. The answer distinguishes between past, present, and future as a necessary distinction. The present is defined as that which exists and is neither past nor future. The 'now' fits this definition, so it is present. The regress does not occur because 'present' does not mean being contained in something else.

Correction: The author defines 'present' as that which exists and is neither past nor future; no container is required, thus no infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The author defines 'the present' as existence without past/future status, not as being contained in something, thus avoiding infinite regress.

The Fourth Objection to the Definition of Motion
The author presents the fourth objection to the definition of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection to defining motion as a series of successive occurrences in contiguous spaces.

Core Idea
Motion cannot be reduced to occurrences in space; it requires a transitional state.

The author presents a key objection that challenges the definition of motion as mere spatial positions. This objection argues that if motion were just a series of occurrences, then there must be an intermediate state between positions, which suggests motion is a prior state. Let's read the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic text of the fourth objection carefully. Notice how it argues that if motion is just occurrences, then there must be a transitional state.
2
Identify the premise
The objection assumes that during motion, the substance is either in the first space (not moving) or in the second (motion complete). Identify this dichotomy.
This is a key point.
3
Consider the conclusion
The objection concludes that motion is a state preceding occurrence in space. Why does that follow?
Think about the implication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع : إن دل ما ذكرتم على أن الحركة عبارة عن حصولات متعاقبة ، في أحياز متلاصقة . فههنا دليل آخر يبطله . وذلك لأن الجوهر إذا انتقل من حيز إلى حيز آخر ، فهو ما دام يكون باقياً في الحيز الأول ، فهو بعد لم يتحرك . وإذا أوصل إلى الحيز الثاني، فقد تمت الحركة وانقرضت . فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : إنه إنما يكون متحركاً فيها بين الحالتين ، وذلك يدل على أن الحركة ليست عبارة عن الحصول في الحيز ، بل الحركة عبارة عن حالة متقدمة على الحصول في الحيز [والله أعلم (۱) ] .
والجواب عن السؤال الأول : أن نقول : [ إن (۲) ] التفرقة بين الماضي وبين المستقبل وبين الحال : معلوم بالضرورة . فنحن نعني بالحاضر : الموجود الذي لا يكون ماضياً ولا مستقبلاً . ولا شك أن الآن الحاضر كذلك ، فيكون الآن (۳) حاضراً ولا نعني بالحاضر أن يكون مظروفاً في شيء ، وأن يكون حاصلاً في شيء آخر ، حتى يلزمنا التسلسل
.
وعن السؤال الثاني : أن نقول : ذلك الجزء الحاضر، الحاصل من الحركة . إن بقي فقد صار الجسم ساكناً ، وانقطعت الحركة.. وإن لم يبق البتة ، إلا في ذلك الآن الحاضر، فلا بد وأن يحصل عقيبه شيء آخر ، هو عند حصوله يكون أيضاً حاضراً حاصلاً . وحينئذ يظهر أنه لا متوسط بينهما .
.
وعن [ السؤال (٤) ] الثالث : وهو قوله : « لم لا يجوز أن يكون الحاصل في الحال من الحركة [ منقسماً ، ويحصل ] جزء دفعة () ؟ ، أن نقول : إنه لا يتعلق غرضنا بنفي (٦) هذا النوع من قبول القسمة . بل نقول : إما أن يمكن أن يحصل في ذلك الشيء الحاضر جزءان ، بحيث يكون أحدهما سابقاً على الآخر بحسب الزمان أو لا يمكن . فإن حصل هذا النوع من القسمة ، لم يكن المجموع حاصلاً . وكلامنا في الحاصل . هذا خلف . وإن امتنع حصول هذا النوع من القسمة ، كان المقصود حاصلاً ، لأن القدر (۱) المتحرك عليه من المسافة بذلك القدر من الحركة. إن كان منقسماً ، كانت الحركة إلى نصفه سابقة بالزمان على الحركة من نصفه ، إلى آخره [ فحينئذ (٣) ] يفرض في ذلك القدر من الحركة جزءان ، بحيث يكون أحدهما سابقاً على الآخر بالزمان . وقد فرضنا : أنه ليس كذلك. هذا خلف . وإن لم يكن ذلك القدر من المسافة قابلا للقسمة ، فذلك هو الجوهر الفرد. فثبت : أن مقصودنا في هذا الدليل يتم ببيان أن القدر الحاضر من الحركة، لا يقبل القسمة التي يكون أحد الجزءين سابقاً على الآخر
وعن السؤال الرابع : وهو قوله : ( الحركة عبارة عن الانتقال من حيز إلى حيز ، وليست عبارة عن الحصول في الحيز ، فنقول : إنه حال كونه منتقلا من حيز إلى حيز ، فإما أن يكون حاصلا في حيز معين . وذلك يبطل قولكم ه إن كونه متحركاً ، ينافي كونه حاصلاً في حيز معين ) . وإما أن يكون حاصلاً في حيز غير (۳) معين . أو يقال : إنه غير حاصل في شيء من الأحياز. والعلم بفساد كل واحد من هذين القسمين : علم ضروري فزال السؤال [ والله
أعلم (1) ] .
The fourth question: If what you mentioned indicates that motion is a series of successive occurrences in contiguous spaces, then here is another argument that invalidates it. That is because if the substance moves from one space to another space, as long as it remains in the first space, it has not yet moved. And when it reaches the second space, the motion is completed and has ceased. So nothing remains except to say that it is moving only in between the two states, and that indicates that motion is not an occurrence in space, but rather motion is a state preceding the occurrence in space [and Allah knows best].

The answer to the first question: We say: The distinction between the past, the future, and the present is known necessarily. So we mean by 'present': the existent that is neither past nor future. And there is no doubt that the present moment is like that, so it is present. And we do not mean by 'present' that it is contained in something, that it occurs in something else, so that a regress would be necessary.

And regarding the second question: We say: That present part, obtained from the motion. If it remains, then the body has become stationary, and the motion has ceased. And if it does not remain at all, except in that present moment, then it is necessary that something else occurs after it, which at the time of its occurrence is also present and obtained. Then it becomes apparent that there is no intermediate between them.

And regarding the third question: which is his saying: 'Why is it not permissible that what is obtained in the present moment of motion is divisible, and a part occurs instantaneously?' We say: Our purpose is not to negate this type of divisibility. Rather, we say: Either it is possible that two parts occur in that present thing such that one precedes the other in time, or not. If this type of division occurs, then the sum is not obtained. And our statement is about what is obtained. This is a contradiction. And if this type of division is impossible, then the objective is achieved, because the amount of distance traversed by that amount of motion: if it is divisible, then the motion to its half precedes in time the motion from its half to its end. Then in that amount of motion, two parts are assumed, such that one precedes the other in time. And we have assumed that it is not so. This is a contradiction. And if that amount of distance is not divisible, then that is the indivisible substance (al-jawhar al-fard). So it is established that our objective in this argument is achieved by demonstrating that the present amount of motion does not accept division such that one of the two parts precedes the other.

And regarding the fourth question: which is his saying: 'Motion is the transition from space to space, and it is not the occurrence in space.' We say: In the state of his transitioning from space to space, either he is occurring in a specific space. This invalidates your statement that his being in motion contradicts his being in a specific space. Or he is occurring in an unspecified space. Or it is said that he is not occurring in any of the spaces. Knowledge of the corruption of each of these two divisions is necessary knowledge. So the objection is removed. [And Allah knows best.]
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection to the definition of motion as successive positions in contiguous spaces, and then provides answers to all four objections (though the first three objections themselves are not given in the text). The author begins by stating the fourth objection: if motion is defined as a series of occurrences in contiguous spaces, then a substance moving from one space to another is either remaining in the first (not moving) or has reached the second (motion complete), so there is only a transitional state between them, which suggests motion is a state prior to occurrence in space. Then the author answers each objection. The answer to the first objection clarifies that the distinction between past, present, and future is known necessarily, and 'present' does not imply being contained in something else to avoid regress. The answer to the second objection argues that if a part of motion in the present remains, motion stops; if it does not remain, something else must follow immediately, showing no intermediate state. The answer to the third objection deals with divisibility: the present part of motion cannot be divided such that one part precedes the other in time, because that would imply a temporal sequence within an indivisible moment, leading to contradiction; this argument supports atomism (al-jawhar al-fard). The answer to the fourth objection shows that a transitioning substance must be either in a specific space (contradicting the claim that motion precludes being in a specific space), in an unspecified space, or in no space at all, all of which are absurd by necessary knowledge. Thus the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
حركة
حصولات متعاقبة
أحياز متلاصقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth objection, what is motion?
A) A series of successive occurrences in contiguous spaces
B) A state preceding the occurrence in space
C) The substance's existence in a specific space
D) The cessation of occurrence

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection argues that if motion were just occurrences in spaces, a moving substance would never actually be in motion, so motion must be a prior state.

Answers to the First Three Objections
The author responds to three objections about the definition of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's responses to three objections regarding the present moment, the continuity of motion, and its divisibility.

Core Idea
The present moment is undivided and motion does not require a temporal gap.

The author now answers three objections. The first clarifies the concept of 'present' to avoid regress. The second argues against an intermediate state. The third addresses divisibility: the present part of motion cannot be divided in time. These answers defend the definition of motion as successive occurrences.

Guided Path
1
Read the three answers
Read the Arabic text for answers to the first, second, and third questions. Notice the logical structure of each.
2
Understand the first answer
The author distinguishes past, present, future by necessary knowledge. The present is not contained in something else.
3
Understand the second answer
If a part of motion remains, motion stops; if not, another part follows immediately. No intermediate.
4
Understand the third answer
The present part of motion cannot have temporal parts; otherwise contradiction. This supports atomism.
Key argument for indivisible substance.

السؤال الرابع : إن دل ما ذكرتم على أن الحركة عبارة عن حصولات متعاقبة ، في أحياز متلاصقة . فههنا دليل آخر يبطله . وذلك لأن الجوهر إذا انتقل من حيز إلى حيز آخر ، فهو ما دام يكون باقياً في الحيز الأول ، فهو بعد لم يتحرك . وإذا أوصل إلى الحيز الثاني، فقد تمت الحركة وانقرضت . فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : إنه إنما يكون متحركاً فيها بين الحالتين ، وذلك يدل على أن الحركة ليست عبارة عن الحصول في الحيز ، بل الحركة عبارة عن حالة متقدمة على الحصول في الحيز [والله أعلم (۱) ] .
والجواب عن السؤال الأول : أن نقول : [ إن (۲) ] التفرقة بين الماضي وبين المستقبل وبين الحال : معلوم بالضرورة . فنحن نعني بالحاضر : الموجود الذي لا يكون ماضياً ولا مستقبلاً . ولا شك أن الآن الحاضر كذلك ، فيكون الآن (۳) حاضراً ولا نعني بالحاضر أن يكون مظروفاً في شيء ، وأن يكون حاصلاً في شيء آخر ، حتى يلزمنا التسلسل
.
وعن السؤال الثاني : أن نقول : ذلك الجزء الحاضر، الحاصل من الحركة . إن بقي فقد صار الجسم ساكناً ، وانقطعت الحركة.. وإن لم يبق البتة ، إلا في ذلك الآن الحاضر، فلا بد وأن يحصل عقيبه شيء آخر ، هو عند حصوله يكون أيضاً حاضراً حاصلاً . وحينئذ يظهر أنه لا متوسط بينهما .
.
وعن [ السؤال (٤) ] الثالث : وهو قوله : « لم لا يجوز أن يكون الحاصل في الحال من الحركة [ منقسماً ، ويحصل ] جزء دفعة () ؟ ، أن نقول : إنه لا يتعلق غرضنا بنفي (٦) هذا النوع من قبول القسمة . بل نقول : إما أن يمكن أن يحصل في ذلك الشيء الحاضر جزءان ، بحيث يكون أحدهما سابقاً على الآخر بحسب الزمان أو لا يمكن . فإن حصل هذا النوع من القسمة ، لم يكن المجموع حاصلاً . وكلامنا في الحاصل . هذا خلف . وإن امتنع حصول هذا النوع من القسمة ، كان المقصود حاصلاً ، لأن القدر (۱) المتحرك عليه من المسافة بذلك القدر من الحركة. إن كان منقسماً ، كانت الحركة إلى نصفه سابقة بالزمان على الحركة من نصفه ، إلى آخره [ فحينئذ (٣) ] يفرض في ذلك القدر من الحركة جزءان ، بحيث يكون أحدهما سابقاً على الآخر بالزمان . وقد فرضنا : أنه ليس كذلك. هذا خلف . وإن لم يكن ذلك القدر من المسافة قابلا للقسمة ، فذلك هو الجوهر الفرد. فثبت : أن مقصودنا في هذا الدليل يتم ببيان أن القدر الحاضر من الحركة، لا يقبل القسمة التي يكون أحد الجزءين سابقاً على الآخر
وعن السؤال الرابع : وهو قوله : ( الحركة عبارة عن الانتقال من حيز إلى حيز ، وليست عبارة عن الحصول في الحيز ، فنقول : إنه حال كونه منتقلا من حيز إلى حيز ، فإما أن يكون حاصلا في حيز معين . وذلك يبطل قولكم ه إن كونه متحركاً ، ينافي كونه حاصلاً في حيز معين ) . وإما أن يكون حاصلاً في حيز غير (۳) معين . أو يقال : إنه غير حاصل في شيء من الأحياز. والعلم بفساد كل واحد من هذين القسمين : علم ضروري فزال السؤال [ والله
أعلم (1) ] .
The fourth question: If what you mentioned indicates that motion is a series of successive occurrences in contiguous spaces, then here is another argument that invalidates it. That is because if the substance moves from one space to another space, as long as it remains in the first space, it has not yet moved. And when it reaches the second space, the motion is completed and has ceased. So nothing remains except to say that it is moving only in between the two states, and that indicates that motion is not an occurrence in space, but rather motion is a state preceding the occurrence in space [and Allah knows best].

The answer to the first question: We say: The distinction between the past, the future, and the present is known necessarily. So we mean by 'present': the existent that is neither past nor future. And there is no doubt that the present moment is like that, so it is present. And we do not mean by 'present' that it is contained in something, that it occurs in something else, so that a regress would be necessary.

And regarding the second question: We say: That present part, obtained from the motion. If it remains, then the body has become stationary, and the motion has ceased. And if it does not remain at all, except in that present moment, then it is necessary that something else occurs after it, which at the time of its occurrence is also present and obtained. Then it becomes apparent that there is no intermediate between them.

And regarding the third question: which is his saying: 'Why is it not permissible that what is obtained in the present moment of motion is divisible, and a part occurs instantaneously?' We say: Our purpose is not to negate this type of divisibility. Rather, we say: Either it is possible that two parts occur in that present thing such that one precedes the other in time, or not. If this type of division occurs, then the sum is not obtained. And our statement is about what is obtained. This is a contradiction. And if this type of division is impossible, then the objective is achieved, because the amount of distance traversed by that amount of motion: if it is divisible, then the motion to its half precedes in time the motion from its half to its end. Then in that amount of motion, two parts are assumed, such that one precedes the other in time. And we have assumed that it is not so. This is a contradiction. And if that amount of distance is not divisible, then that is the indivisible substance (al-jawhar al-fard). So it is established that our objective in this argument is achieved by demonstrating that the present amount of motion does not accept division such that one of the two parts precedes the other.

And regarding the fourth question: which is his saying: 'Motion is the transition from space to space, and it is not the occurrence in space.' We say: In the state of his transitioning from space to space, either he is occurring in a specific space. This invalidates your statement that his being in motion contradicts his being in a specific space. Or he is occurring in an unspecified space. Or it is said that he is not occurring in any of the spaces. Knowledge of the corruption of each of these two divisions is necessary knowledge. So the objection is removed. [And Allah knows best.]
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection to the definition of motion as successive positions in contiguous spaces, and then provides answers to all four objections (though the first three objections themselves are not given in the text). The author begins by stating the fourth objection: if motion is defined as a series of occurrences in contiguous spaces, then a substance moving from one space to another is either remaining in the first (not moving) or has reached the second (motion complete), so there is only a transitional state between them, which suggests motion is a state prior to occurrence in space. Then the author answers each objection. The answer to the first objection clarifies that the distinction between past, present, and future is known necessarily, and 'present' does not imply being contained in something else to avoid regress. The answer to the second objection argues that if a part of motion in the present remains, motion stops; if it does not remain, something else must follow immediately, showing no intermediate state. The answer to the third objection deals with divisibility: the present part of motion cannot be divided such that one part precedes the other in time, because that would imply a temporal sequence within an indivisible moment, leading to contradiction; this argument supports atomism (al-jawhar al-fard). The answer to the fourth objection shows that a transitioning substance must be either in a specific space (contradicting the claim that motion precludes being in a specific space), in an unspecified space, or in no space at all, all of which are absurd by necessary knowledge. Thus the objection is refuted.

Answer to the Fourth Objection
The author refutes the fourth objection using a trilemma argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the fourth objection using necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
A moving substance must be in a specific space, contradicting the claim that motion precludes being in a space.

Having answered three objections, the author now addresses the fourth objection that motion is a transitional state. He argues that if a substance is moving, it must be either in a specific space (contradicting the objection), an unspecified space, or no space at all—all impossible by necessary knowledge. Thus the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer to the fourth objection
Read the Arabic text. The author presents a trilemma: the moving substance is either in a specific space, in an unspecified space, or in no space at all.
2
Analyze the trilemma
Consider each horn: if in a specific space, that contradicts the objection's claim; if in unspecified or no space, these are known to be false.
3
Conclusion
The objection is removed because all possibilities lead to absurdity or contradiction.

The fourth question: If what you mentioned indicates that motion is a series of successive occurrences in contiguous spaces, then here is another argument that invalidates it. That is because if the substance moves from one space to another space, as long as it remains in the first space, it has not yet moved. Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection to the definition of motion as successive positions in contiguous spaces, and then provides answers to all four objections (though the first three objections themselves are not given in the text). Second Proof: The Cube's Bounding Surfaces
Sanusi begins the second proof using a cube's finite bounding surfaces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the geometry of a cube to begin arguing that motion consists of discrete successive positions.

Core Idea
A cube's finite boundaries hint at indivisible atoms underlying motion.

The second proof for the atomistic view of motion starts with a simple geometric observation: a cube has a finite number of bounding surfaces and lines. This observation is used to establish the existence of indivisible units (atoms) in space, which then supports the idea that motion occurs through discrete jumps between contiguous positions. In this lesson, we examine the opening of that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the geometric terms: cube, surfaces, lines, finite.
The text breaks off mid-sentence; the rest of the proof continues in later pages.
2
Identify the geometric structure
The cube has six surfaces, each surface is bounded by four lines. This finite enumeration is key to the argument.
3
Understand the proof's aim
The proof aims to use finite boundaries to infer indivisible points (atoms). Motion across discrete space then consists of successive positions.
This argument is typical of kalam atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second argument to demonstrate that motion is a series of successive occurrences in contiguous spaces: We say: The cubic body is surrounded by six finite surfaces, and each of those surfaces is surrounded by four finite lines, and each of those...

Commentary
This passage starts the second proof for the definition of motion as successive positions in contiguous spaces. The argument uses a geometric example: a cubic body has six finite bounding surfaces; each surface is bounded by four finite lines. The proof is incomplete in the text, but the implication is that the boundaries imply indivisible points or atoms, which supports the idea of motion as discrete steps.

The Third Proof: Motion Consists of Indivisible Successive Occurrences
The author proves that motion consists of successive indivisible instants by analyzing extension and existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third kalam argument that motion is composed of discrete, indivisible instants, and why the continuous view is rationally untenable.

Core Idea
Motion is not a continuous whole; only the present instant exists and is indivisible.

The author presents a third proof against the continuous conception of motion. He begins by noting that motion appears extended but only the present part actually exists. He then shows that the present part itself cannot be extended, otherwise it would have the same problem. Hence motion must be a series of indivisible moments. The opposing view that motion is a single continuous divisible thing is shown to be absurd because it would connect two non-existents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the complete Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the flow of the argument settle before you analyse it.
Pay attention to the phrase 'الحاصل في الحال' (the present occurrence) – it is central.
2
Follow the logical dissection
Notice how the author breaks down motion: first the whole extended thing is not existent in reality, only a part exists at present. Then he analyses that present part.
He uses the same reasoning on the part as on the whole.
3
Understand the conclusion about indivisible parts
Once it is established that the present part lacks extension and divisibility, the author concludes that motion is a series of such indivisible occurrences succeeding each other.
This is the 'successive occurrences' – الحصولات المتعاقبة.
4
Reflect on the rebuttal
The author refutes the continuous view by showing it would connect non-existents. Consider why this is absurd and how it strengthens the proof.
The key point: past and future are non-existent, so a continuous motion would join non-existence to non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة في إثبات هذا المطلوب : أن نقول : الحركة أمر ممتد من أول المسافة إلى آخرها . ولا شك أن ذلك الأمر الممتد لا وجود له في الأعيان . إنما الموجود في الأعيان جزء من أجزائها ، وقسم من أقسامها ، وهو الحاصل في الحال . وأما الماضي والمستقبل فهما معدومان . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : ذلك الحاصل الحاضر. إما أن يكون له امتداد، أو لا يكون . فإن كان الأول ، فحينئذ لا يكون هو أيضاً موجوداً في الأعيان ، لعين ما ذكرناه في كلية الحركة . وإذا بطل هذا ، ثبت : أن الحاضر الحاصل في الحال ، ليس فيه امتداد ، ولا قبول قسمة البتة . وعند فنائه يحصل شيء آخر ، حاله كذلك . وعلى هذا التقدير ، فالحركة تكترن عبارة عن حصولات متعاقبة ، بحيث يكون كل واحد منها غير قابل للقسمة أصلا . وذلك هو المطلوب . وقول من يقول : الحركة شيء واحد، متصل من أول المسافة إلى آخرها ، وهو يقبل القسمة إلى غير النهاية : قول معلوم البطلان بالبديهة ، عند استحضار تصورات هذه القضية . لأن الماضي معدوم ، والمستقبل أيضاً معدوم . فالقول بكون الحركة شيئاً واحداً متصلاً ، يوجب كون أحد العدمين متصلا بعدم آخر ، بطرف موجود . وهو الجزء الحاضر من الحركة . واتصال العدم المحض بالعدم المحض قول لا يقبله العقل البتة
The third proof for establishing this sought conclusion: we say: motion is an extended thing from the beginning of the distance to its end. There is no doubt that this extended thing has no existence in external individuals. Rather, what exists in external individuals is a part of its parts and a division of its divisions, which is what occurs in the present moment. As for the past and the future, they are non-existent. If this is established, we say: that present occurrence either has extension or does not. If it is the former, then it too is not existent in external individuals for the very same reason we mentioned regarding motion as a whole. When this is invalidated, it is established that the present occurrence in the current moment has no extension and no divisibility at all. Upon its passing away, another thing obtains with the same state. On this basis, motion becomes an expression of successive occurrences such that each one is absolutely indivisible. That is the sought conclusion. The statement of whoever says that motion is a single continuous thing from the beginning of the distance to its end, and that it is divisible infinitely, is a statement known to be false by necessity upon considering the concepts of this proposition. Because the past is non-existent, and the future is also non-existent. So saying that motion is a single continuous thing necessitates connecting one non-existent to another non-existent with an existent endpoint—namely, the present part of motion. The connection of pure non-existence to pure non-existence is a statement the intellect absolutely does not accept.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a proof (third argument) that motion is composed of discrete, indivisible instants. The author begins by defining motion as an extended continuum from start to finish of a distance. He then argues that this extended whole does not exist in external reality; only a part of it exists at any given moment—the present part. The past and future are non-existent. He then analyzes the present part: if it had extension, it would suffer the same problem as the whole (only part of it existing), so it must be extensionless and indivisible. When it ceases, the next part appears likewise. Hence motion is a series of successive indivisible occurrences. This is the desired conclusion. He then rebuts the opposing view that motion is a single continuous divisible entity. He argues that this view is self-evidently false because it would connect two non-existents (past and future) to an existent present part, and connecting pure non-existence to non-existence is intellectually unacceptable.

الممتد
الحاصل في الحال
العدم
الحصولات المتعاقبة
غير قابل للقسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Motion is a single continuous entity that can be divided infinitely.
Correction: Motion is actually a series of discrete, indivisible present occurrences; the continuous view is false because it would connect non-existent past and future.

Variation of Attributes Proves Actual Distinct Instants
Concluding the first type: motion requires successive instants with different attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the variation of attributes during motion necessitates that instants are actually distinct, not merely potentially distinct.

Core Idea
Motion's changing attributes reveal that time instants are truly distinct.

The author concludes the first argument by showing that in motion, attributes change successively, each instant holding a unique attribute. This observation forces us to accept that instants are actually different, not just potentially. Let's examine the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

The passage is short but dense; focus on the logical connection between differing attributes and actual variation.
2

The phrase 'على سبيل الفعل، لا على سبيل القوة' is crucial.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction matters: if instants were only potentially distinct, motion would not require real change. This solidifies the reality of time as composed of discrete instants.

The Existence of the Instant and Two Proofs
Second objection and two proofs for the existence of the instant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of an instant is proven by considering God's temporal existence and the impossibility of circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
An instant must exist because God is described as existing now.

This short passage tackles a subtle objection: if a thing exists in an instant, does that instant itself exist? The author argues yes, and gives two clear proofs. You'll see how divine attributes and logical reasoning converge to establish the reality of temporal moments.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and question
Start by reading the objection that is introduced: 'We grant that a thing exists in the instant. Why do you say that if this is true, it necessitates that the instant exists?' Notice the precise question being asked.
The objection does not deny existence in an instant, but questions whether the instant itself exists.
2
Examine the first proof
Look at the first proof: God is described as existing 'now' in the instant and time. This assumes the instant is a real container. Ask yourself why this proves the instant's existence.
The proof relies on God's attribute of being temporally present.
3
Examine the second proof
The second proof considers the instant itself. It argues that the instant cannot exist 'in itself' (circular) nor can there be an infinite regress of instants. Both are impossible, so the instant must exist distinctly.
This proof uses logical contradiction to show the instant's independent existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : سلمنا أن الشيء وجد في الآن . قلم قلتم : إن صدق هذا ، يقتضي كون الآن موجوداً ؟ والدليل عليه : وجهان : الأول : إنه تعالى يصدق عليه أنه موجود الآن في الآن والزمان . والثاني : إن نفس الآن موجود ، مع أنه يمتنع أن يقال : إن الآن وجد في الآن . إلا لزم كون الشيء : موجوداً في نفسه ، أو يلزم التسلسل . وكلاهما محال
The second question: We grant that a thing exists in the instant. Why do you say that if this is true, it necessitates that the instant exists? The evidence for it is two aspects: First: God Most High is truly described as existing now, in the instant and time. Second: The instant itself exists, while it is impossible to say that the instant exists in the instant, for that would entail either the thing existing in itself or infinite regress. Both are impossible.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage handles a second objection: assuming a thing exists in an instant, does that imply the instant itself exists? The author provides two proofs. First, God is said to exist 'now' in the instant and time, so the instant must exist as a container. Second, the instant itself exists, but we cannot say the instant exists in itself (since that would be circular) nor can we have an infinite regress of instants containing other instants. Both are impossible, so the instant must exist as a distinct entity.

Misconception: The existence of an instant is self-evident and doesn't need proof.
Correction: The objection challenges this, and the author proves it using God's temporal existence and the logical impossibility of infinite regress or circularity.

Checkpoint

Time as a Discrete Quantity: The Succession of Instants
Concluding argument that time is a discrete quantity composed of successive units.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that time is a discrete quantity composed of successive instants, based on the impossibility of connecting non-existent entities through an existent.

Core Idea
Time is discrete, composed of successive units, not a continuous flow.

In this passage, the author presents a concise argument against the continuity of time. By examining what it means for time to be a container shared between past and future, he shows that a continuous model leads to an absurdity, thus proving that time must be composed of discrete atomic instants.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's logical flow come through.
Note the key terms: زمان, آن, كم منفصل.
2
Identify the reductio structure
Notice how the author sets up a premise (time as shared container), derives an impossible consequence (connecting non-existents), and concludes the opposite (discrete quantity).
This is a classic kalam argument form.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that time being discrete means it is composed of indivisible units (instants). Contemplate how this view differs from the continuous view of time.
The author notes a detailed version in another chapter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما هو ظرف مشترك فيه بين الماضي وبين المستقبل. لأنها نهاية الماضي وبداية المستقبل. وهذا القول يقتضي اتصال معدوم بمعدوم آخر، بطرف موجود. وإنه محال. فثبت: أنه كم منفصل. فهو مركب من وحدات متعاقبة، وذلك يوجب تتالي الآنات. وتقرير هذا الوجه: مذكور في باب الآن والزمان على الاستقصاء.
And it is only a container shared between the past and the future, because it is the end of the past and the beginning of the future. And this statement necessitates the connection of a non-existent with another non-existent by means of an existent extremity. And that is impossible. So it is established that it is a discrete quantity. So it is composed of successive units, and that necessitates the succession of instants. And the elaboration of this aspect is mentioned in the chapter on the instant and time in detail.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage concludes an argument about the nature of time. The author presents a reductio ad absurdum: if time were continuous, it would serve as a container connecting the past (non-existent) and the future (non-existent) through the present (existent). This is impossible because a non-existent cannot be connected to another non-existent through an existent in a continuous manner. Therefore, time must be a discrete quantity (كم منفصل), composed of successive atomic units (وحدات متعاقبة), leading necessarily to the succession of instants (تتالي الآنات). The author notes that a detailed exposition of this argument is found in the chapter on the instant and time.

Key Terms
زمان
آن
كم منفصل
تتالي الآنات
معدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is continuous and infinitely divisible.
Correction: The argument shows that continuity leads to an absurdity, so time must be discrete and composed of indivisible units (instants).

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of this passage?
A) Time is continuous.
B) Time is discrete and composed of successive units.
C) The past and future are connected by an existent.
D) Time is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
Time is a discrete quantity composed of successive atomic units (instants), because a continuous time would require an impossible connection of non-existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is composed of discrete instants, each 'now' is an unrepeatable gift. Are you aware that your past sins are gone and your future is not yet written? How will you seize this very instant to repent or perform an act of obedience?
?
The argument shows that time cannot connect past and future—meaning you have no access to yesterday or tomorrow, only the present. Do you live as if each moment is your only chance? What one action will you do right now to draw closer to Allah?
?
Time's discrete nature means your life is a series of independent moments, each accountable. Are you treating each moment as a separate deed? What will you change in your next instant to ensure it is spent in mindfulness of Allah?

The Fourth Proof for the Succession of Instants: Razi's Adaptation of Ibn Sina's Argument
Razi presents the fourth proof for tatali al-anat, adapting Ibn Sina's stone argument and applying it to celestial motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi adapts Ibn Sina's stone argument into a forceful proof for the succession of instants by applying it to celestial motion.

Core Idea
Celestial motion forces a choice: rest or successive instants.

Razi takes Ibn Sina's argument about a stone's moment of rest at the apex of its flight and turns it against the philosophers. By applying the same logic to the sphere of Saturn at the apogee of its deferent, he forces a dilemma: either celestial motions are interrupted by a rest (which philosophers reject) or time is composed of indivisible instants (tatali al-anat). This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice that the argument has multiple layers: a claim, an objection, and a counter-objection.
2
Identify the dilemma in the stone argument
Ibn Sina claims that at the apex of its flight, the stone must have a moment of rest to avoid successive instants. Understand why he says that.
3
Follow the wheel objection
Ibn Sina raises an objection where a wheel would need to stop if contact and non-contact occur in two instants. He accepts this consequence.
4
See how Razi turns the tables
Razi uses the example of Saturn touching the sphere of fixed stars. The same reasoning leads to either a rest (which halts celestial motion) or tatali al-anat.
5
Reconstruct the proof form
Razi reformulates the argument as a clear proof: contact and non-contact occur in two consecutive instants, so instants follow each other without time gaps.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة على إثبات تتالي الآنات: أن نقول: إن الشيخ أبا علي بن سينا ادعى أن الحجر إذا رمي إلى فوق، فإنه لا بد وأن يحصل بين حركته الصاعدة وحركته الهابطة سكون في المواء. واحتج عليه: بأن القوة التي تحرك ذلك الحجر إلى فوق، إنما تحرك لأجل أن توصله إلى حد معين من حدود المسافة، وذلك الحد طرف غير منقسم، فيكون الوصول إليه واقعاً في الآن، ثم إنه يصير مماساً عندما يبتدئ بالنزول. وذلك إنما يقع في آن. ثم قال: ويجب أن يحصل بين الآنين زمان، حتى لا يلزم تتالي الآنات. فوجب أن يكون الحجر ساكناً في ذلك الزمان. ثم إنه أورد على نفسه سؤالاً فقال: إذا فرضنا دولاباً وعلقنا عليه كرة، وكان فوق ذلك الدولاب سطح مستو أملس، بحيث كلما تحرك الدولاب فإن تلك الكرة تلاقي ذلك السطح الفوقاني بنقطة واحدة. فنقول: إن حصول تلك الملاقاة لا بد وأن يقع في الآن، وحصول اللاملاقاة يقع في آن آخر، وبين الآنين زمان فيلزمكم وجوب أن يقف ذلك الدولاب في تلك الحالة. فالتزم الرئيس وجوب هذا السكون. فقلنا: إنا نلزمكم هذا الكلام في صورة لا يمكنكم التزام السكون فيها، وذلك لأنا إذا فرضنا حصول مركز تدوير زحل في أوج فلك التدوير، فههنا قالوا: إن كرة زحل تماس كرة الثوابت بنقطة واحدة. فنقول: إن حصول تلك المماسة يقع في آن لا ينقسم، وحصول اللامماسة يقع في آن أيضاً. فإن حصل بين هذين الآنين زمان السكون، لزمكم انقطاع حركات الأفلاك. وذلك عندكم باطل. وإن لم يحصل هناك سكون متخلل بين الآنين، فحينئذ يلزم تتالي الآنات. وهو المطلوب. إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول: إنا أردنا إيراد هذا الكلام في صورة البرهان قلنا: لا شك أن حصول الملاقاة بين كرة زحل وبين الكرة المكوكبة تقع دفعة في آن. وصيرورة تلك النقطة لا ملاقية ولا مماسة تقع أيضاً في آن. فإن حصل بين الآن زمان يكون زحل فيه ساكناً، لزم انقطاع حركات الكواكب. وهو باطل. وإن لم يحصل هناك هذا الزمان المتخلل، فحينئذ يلزم تتالي الآنات. وهو المطلوب.
The fourth proof for the succession of instants: that we say: Indeed, the master Abu Ali ibn Sina claimed that if a stone is thrown upward, then necessarily between its ascending motion and its descending motion there occurs a rest in the air. And he argued for it: because the force that moves that stone upward only moves it to deliver it to a specific limit of the boundaries of the distance, and that limit is an indivisible extremity, so reaching it occurs in an instant. Then it becomes tangent when it begins to descend. And that only occurs in an instant. Then he said: 'And it is necessary that there occur between the two instants a time, so that the succession of instants does not follow. Thus the stone must be at rest during that time.' Then he raised a question against himself and said: 'If we suppose a wheel, and we hang a sphere on it, and above that wheel there is a smooth flat surface, such that whenever the wheel moves, that sphere contacts that upper surface at a single point. Then we say: The occurrence of that contact must occur in an instant, and the occurrence of non-contact occurs in another instant, and between the two instants there is a time, so you are compelled to the necessity of that wheel stopping in that state.' So the chief (Ibn Sina) committed to the necessity of this rest. Then we said: 'We force upon you this statement in a case in which you cannot commit to rest: that is, if we suppose the occurrence of the center of the epicycle of Saturn at the apogee of the deferent, then here they said: The sphere of Saturn touches the sphere of the fixed stars at a single point. Then we say: The occurrence of that tangency occurs in an indivisible instant, and the occurrence of non-tangency occurs in an instant as well. If there occurs between these two instants a time of rest, you would be compelled to the interruption of the motions of the orbs, and that is false according to you. And if there does not occur a rest interposed between the two instants, then the succession of instants follows. And that is what is sought.' If you know this, then we say: we intended to present this statement in the form of a proof. We said: There is no doubt that the occurrence of contact between the sphere of Saturn and the starry sphere occurs simultaneously in an instant. And the becoming of that point as non-contacting and non-tangent also occurs in an instant. If there occurs between the two instants a time during which Saturn is at rest, then the interruption of the motions of the planets follows, and that is false. And if there does not occur this interposed time, then the succession of instants follows. And that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for the succession of instants (تتالي الآنات). It begins with Ibn Sina's argument about a stone thrown upward: at the apex of its flight, it must have a moment of rest between ascending and descending, because the limit point is indivisible and reached in an instant. To avoid a succession of instants (i.e., no time gap), Ibn Sina posits a period of rest between the two instants. He then raises a self-objection using a wheel with a sphere contacting a smooth surface: if contact and non-contact occur in two instants, there must be a time gap of rest, causing the wheel to stop—which seems absurd, but Ibn Sina accepts the consequence. The author (Razi) then turns the tables using an astronomical example: the sphere of Saturn touches the sphere of fixed stars at a single point at apogee. The same reasoning forces either a rest (which would halt planetary motion, unacceptable to philosophers) or the acceptance of tatali al-anat. The argument is reformulated as a clear proof: contact and non-contact occur in two consecutive instants with no time between them, leading inevitably to the succession of instants.

Key Terms

آن
سكون
حركة
مماسة
مركز تدوير
جوهر فرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that celestial bodies actually stop at apogee.
Correction: The argument is hypothetical: it shows that if there were a time gap, motion would stop; since motion doesn't stop, the gap must be absent, implying consecutive instants.

Checkpoint
What does the example of Saturn's epicycle demonstrate?
A) That celestial motions require continuous time.
B) That either celestial motion stops or tatali al-anat follows.
C) That Saturn is stationary at apogee.
D) That the philosophers accept tatali al-anat.

Key Takeaway
Razi's fourth proof forces the acceptance of tatali al-anat by showing that denying it would require celestial motions to stop, which is impossible.

Razi's proof shows that even the heavens cannot have a moment of rest without breaking their motion. In your spiritual life, are there 'rests'—times where you stagnate and fail to move closer to Allah? What would it take for you to accept that there is no time for rest in your journey to Him?
