First Proof for the Indivisible Atom (Two Faces)
Razi presents the first proof with two faces for the indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of Razi's first proof for the indivisible atom, including its two faces and how they reject objections.

Core Idea
Razi's proof shows that points must be adjacent to avoid the absurd leap (ṭafra).

This passage presents a foundational proof for the indivisible atom, addressing an objection about gaps between two points. Razi provides two complementary logical arguments ('two faces') to show that a line must be composed of directly adjacent points, leaving no room for empty space or a leap. Let's examine each face carefully.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first two sentences: the proof states the desired claim and then raises an objection: 'Why did you say that no line occurred between those two points?' This frames the problem.
The objection challenges the claim that a line is composed of points.
2
Analyze the first face
The first face considers two possibilities: a point or a line between the two points. Follow each branch and see why both lead to the conclusion of adjacent points.
This face eliminates the possibility of a line between points.
3
Analyze the second face
The second face argues using instantaneous contact and non-contact. Notice how it deduces that two points must be adjacent at the moment non-contact begins.
This face reinforces the conclusion without relying on the first face.
4
Synthesize the proof
Combine both faces: they arrive at the same conclusion that the line is composed of points. Reflect on why both arguments are needed.
Razi provides multiple arguments to strengthen the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالنقطة التي لا تتجزا ، فيكون ذلك الخط مؤلفاً من النقط وهو المطلوب قوله : لم قلتم : إنه لم يحصل بين تينك النقطتين خط ؟ قلنا : تقريره من وجهين : الأول : إن هاتين النقطتين ، إما أن يتخللهما نقطة أو خط . فإن كان الأول فقد حصل تشافع النقط . وإن كان الثاني ، فإما أن يقال : الكرة ماست السطح بذلك المستقيم ، أو لم تحصل المماسة على ذلك المستقيم . والأول باطل . وإلا لعاد ما ذكرنا من أن موضع المماسة يكون منقسماً . والثاني باطل · وإلا لزم أن يقال : الكرة ماست السطح بالنقطة الأولى ، ثم ماسته بالنقطة الثانية ، مع أنه لم تحصل المماسة بما بينهما . وذلك هو القول بالطفرة . وإنه باطل . الوجه الثاني في تقرير هذا المطلوب : أن نقول : الملاقاة بالنقطة الأولى حصلت دفعة . وكذلك حصول اللاملاقاة بها ، يحصل دفعة ، ثم يستمر في كل الزمان الذي بعده . ففي أول حصول اللاملاقاة بالنقطة الأولى لا شك أنه حصلت الملاقاة بنقطة ثانية . وحينئذ يلزم تشافع هاتين النقطتين . وهو المطلوب .
By the indivisible point, so that line is composed of points, and that is the desired claim: 'Why did you say that no line occurred between those two points?' We said: Its elaboration is from two faces: First: Those two points are either interspersed by a point or a line. If the former, then juxtaposition of points has occurred. If the latter, then either it is said: The sphere touched the surface by that straight [line], or contact did not occur on that straight [line]. The first is false, otherwise what we mentioned would recur, that the place of contact is divisible. The second is false, otherwise it would necessitate that it be said: The sphere touched the surface at the first point, then touched it at the second point, while contact did not occur between them. That is the claim of leap (ṭafra), and it is false. The second face in elaborating this desired claim: We say: Meeting at the first point occurred instantaneously, and likewise the occurrence of non-meeting at it occurs instantaneously, then it continues in all subsequent time. At the first moment of non-meeting at the first point, there is no doubt that meeting at a second point occurred. Then juxtaposition of these two points is necessitated. And that is the desired claim.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) by addressing an objection: 'Why do you say that no line occurred between two points?' The objection assumes that if a line is composed of points, there should be no gaps, but the opponent claims there is a gap. The proof has two faces. The first face examines the two points: either a point or a line lies between them. If a point lies between, then points are adjacent (tashāfuʿ) — supporting composition. If a line lies between, the sphere either touched that straight line or did not. If it touched, the place of contact would be divisible (contradicting the point). If it did not, then contact occurred only at the first and second points, with no contact in between — this is the leap (ṭafra), which is rejected. The second face: At the first point, contact occurs instantaneously; non-contact also occurs instantaneously and continues. At the first moment of non-contact at the first point, contact at a second point must occur, forcing the two points to be adjacent. Thus the line is composed of points.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
الطفرة
تشافع النقط
الملاقاة
اللاملاقاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes atoms exist as actual parts before the argument.
Correction: It deduces their necessity from the impossibility of contact without them, not from a prior assumption.

Checkpoint
What does the first face of the proof reject as impossible?
A) Juxtaposition of points
B) The sphere touching a straight line
C) The leap (ṭafra) resulting from no contact between two points
D) The line being composed of points

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof establishes that a line must be composed of adjacent indivisible points, using two distinct logical arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi's proof shows that without the assumption of discrete points, logical absurdities follow. Reflect on an area of your life where you have been holding onto a comfortable assumption that, upon closer examination, leads to inconsistency with your faith. What is that assumption, and are you willing to let it go for the sake of truth?
?
The second face of the proof hinges on instantaneous events—contact and non-contact occur in an instant. Consider your own moments of decision: there is an instant when you choose obedience and an instant when you choose negligence. Are you aware of that split second, and how does it affect your accountability before Allah?
?
If every physical line is composed of indivisible points, then the entire material world is built from these simple units. How does this discrete view of creation change your perception of the world's dependence on Allah's continuous act of holding it together? What would it mean for your trust in Him?

Second Proof for the Indivisible Atom: The Moving Line Argument
Razi's second, stronger proof using a moving line on a line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's stronger proof for the indivisible atom using a moving line, and why he considers it superior to the first proof.

Core Idea
Motion requires contact, and contact between points proves lines are composed of indivisible points.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents Razi's second, more robust argument for the indivisible atom. Unlike the earlier proof that relied on circles and spheres, this proof uses a simple geometric thought experiment: a line sliding over another line. As you read, notice how Razi establishes that the moving line's endpoint must touch every part of the base line, leading to the conclusion that the base line is composed of points. The passage ends with a comparative evaluation of the two proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage carefully once, then the translation. Note the key terms.
Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
2
Visualize the thought experiment
Imagine a vertical line moving horizontally across a base line from one end to the other. Ask yourself: what does motion without contact mean?
This is a geometric abstraction.
3
Trace the logical steps
The moving line's endpoint touches the entire base line. The endpoint is a point, so it touches points. Therefore the base line is composed of points.
Follow the implication chain.
4
Understand the comparison
Razi claims this proof is stronger because it does not depend on circles/spheres. Reflect on why that matters for the overall argument for atoms.
The first proof may actually undermine atomism.
5
Connect to the broader debate
This proof supports the existence of indivisible atoms (jawahir fard), a key doctrine in kalam cosmology.
Think about how this fits into the larger theological framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثاني في إثبات الجوهر الفرد : أن نقول : إذا فرضنا خطاً قائماً على خط ، ثم فرضنا الخط القائم، متحركاً على الخط الآخر ، حتى انتهى من أوله إلى آخره. فهذا الخط المتحرك ، قد ماس بطرفه كلية الخط المتحرك عليه . لأن الحركة على الشيء بدون مماسته غير معقول . وهذا يقتضي أن يقال : إن الخط المتحرك عليه ، إنما تولد من أشياء ، ماسها طرف الخط المتحرك . لكن طرف الخط المتحرك نقطة ، والذي تماسه النقطة : نقطة . فالخط المتحرك عليه ، يجب أن يكون مركباً من النقط . وهو المطلوب . واعلم : أن هذا البرهان أقوى وأكمل من الكلام المتقدم . لان الدليل مبني على إثبات الدائرة والكرة . وستعرف : أن إثبات الدائرة والكرة ، يوجب القول بإبطال الجوهر الفرد . وأما هذا البرهان فلا حاجة فيه إلى شيء من المقدمات أصلاً ، وإنه قليل المقدمات ، ظاهر الانتاج .
The second proof for establishing the indivisible atom: We say: If we assume a line standing on a line, then we assume the standing line moving on the other line until it reaches from its beginning to its end. This moving line has touched with its endpoint the entire line on which it moves, because motion upon a thing without touching it is inconceivable. This necessitates that it be said: The line on which it moves is generated from things that the endpoint of the moving line touched. But the endpoint of the moving line is a point, and what a point touches is a point. Therefore the line on which it moves must be composed of points. And that is the desired claim. And know that this proof is stronger and more complete than the previous discussion, because that proof is based on establishing the circle and sphere, and you will know that establishing the circle and sphere necessitates the negation of the indivisible atom. As for this proof, it has no need of any premises at all, and it has few premises and clear entailment.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage provides a second proof for the indivisible atom, which the author claims is stronger than the first. The proof: Assume a line perpendicular to another line, and let the perpendicular line slide across the base line from one end to the other. During this motion, the endpoint of the moving line touches every part of the base line, because motion without contact is impossible. Therefore the base line is composed of parts that the endpoint touches. Since the endpoint is a point (being indivisible), each part it touches must also be a point. Hence the base line is composed of points. The author then compares this proof to the earlier one: the earlier proof depended on the concept of a circle and sphere, which ironically leads to the refutation of the indivisible atom. This new proof is independent, requiring no such premises, and is more straightforward in its conclusion.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
الخط القائم
المماسة
النقطة
الانتاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof assumes that geometric lines are actually composed of discrete points in a physical sense, or that the proof relies on a hidden assumption about the nature of motion.
Correction: The proof is a logical demonstration: if motion occurs, contact is inevitable; if an endpoint (a point) contacts something, that something must also be a point. It does not assume lines are made of points; it deduces that they must be if the thought experiment holds. The proof is independent of any physical theory of motion beyond the impossibility of motion without contact.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the first proof compared to this second proof?
A) The first proof is stronger
B) The second proof is weaker
C) The first proof depends on circles and spheres, which may actually refute the atom
D) Both proofs are equally strong

Key Takeaway
The moving line argument demonstrates that contact between geometric entities forces a point-based composition, providing a stronger proof for the indivisible atom than the earlier circle-based argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi praises this proof for having few premises and clear entailment. Is your faith moving through mere intellectual acknowledgement without ever truly 'touching' your soul? Identify one point of contact (a verse or command) that you have been avoiding. What would it mean for you to allow that point to truly touch you today?

Razi's proof is a model of logical precision. But the most important truths—like the reality of your own death and standing before Allah—do not require geometric proofs. Are you using intellectual complexity as a shield to avoid facing the simple, undeniable truths that demand your immediate action? What is one such truth you have been postponing acting upon? Third Form of the Proof Using Celestial Circles
Razi applies the moving-point proof to the intersection of celestial circles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the intersection of celestial circles to argue for the existence of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
A moving point on the horizon proves lines are made of points.

Razi continues his atomist proofs by applying the moving-point argument to celestial mechanics. He shows that even the regular motion of astronomical circles forces the conclusion that space is composed of discrete points. This lesson walks through the geometric reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the three celestial circles mentioned: equator, ecliptic, and horizon.
2
Identify the argument's logical steps
Trace the argument: the equator and ecliptic rotate point by point. A fixed point on the horizon touches all parts of the equator. Each touch is point-to-point. Therefore the equator consists of points (atoms).
3
Reflect on the atomist conclusion
Consider why this reasoning forces atomism: if a line (the equator) is composed of points, then physical bodies must also be composed of indivisible points, i.e., atoms.
This is a key claim of the kalam atomist school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنه يمكن أن نذكر هذا البرهان في صور كثيرة : الأول : أن نقول : لا شك أن دائرة معدل النهار ، ودائرة فلك البروج ، لا تزال ترتفع نقطة بعد نقطة ، حتى تتم الدورة. وإذا كان كذلك ، فتلك النقطة المعينة من دائرة الأفق ، مست جميع الأجزاء المفترضة في معدل النهار على التعاقب والتلاحق . والذي تمسه النقطة نقطة . فيلزم أن يقال : إن معدل النهار مركب من نقط التماس . وذلك يفيد المطلوب .
And know that it is possible to mention this proof in many forms: The first: We say: There is no doubt that the circle of the celestial equator and the circle of the zodiac are continually rising point after point until the revolution is completed. If that is so, then that specific point from the circle of the horizon touches all the assumed parts of the celestial equator successively and consecutively. And what a point touches is a point. Therefore it necessitates that it be said: The celestial equator is composed of points of contact. And that yields the desired claim.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents a third form of the proof for the indivisible atom, now applied to celestial circles. The author states that the proof can be given in many forms, and this is the first such form. The argument: The celestial equator (dā'irat maʿdal al-nahār) and the ecliptic (dā'irat falak al-burūj) intersect. As they rotate, a specific point on the horizon circle touches the equator at successive points. Since each touch is between two points, the equator must be composed of points. This again forces the conclusion that lines (and thus bodies) are made of indivisible atoms.

Key Terms
دائرة معدل النهار
دائرة فلك البروج
دائرة الأفق
نقط التماس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies on actual astronomical observations of celestial motion.
Correction: The argument uses the logical structure of motion and contact, not empirical data. It is a rational proof for atomism.

Checkpoint
What celestial circles does this proof use?
A) Equator and ecliptic
B) Horizon and meridian
C) Equator and horizon
D) Ecliptic and horizon

Key Takeaway
Razi's third form of the proof shows that even celestial motion forces the conclusion that space is composed of indivisible atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Now consider: In your own daily walk of faith, do you treat your relationship with Allah as a seamless continuum, or do you realize it is built from discrete moments of connection—each prayer, each dhikr, each glance of the heart? What is one 'point of contact' you can improve today to build that chain of atoms?
?
Razi's proof forces you to see the world as made of indivisible atoms—no infinite divisibility. How can this truth reshape your view of your own sins and good deeds? Are they not also discrete 'points' that accumulate to define your soul? Will you let this moment be a point that turns you toward the All-Seeing One, or will you let it vanish into a blur of neglect?
?
The argument hinges on the fact that 'what a point touches is a point.' Reflect: In your interactions with others, do you treat each encounter as a whole, or do you see that every word, every gesture is a tiny 'point' that leaves an impression? How can you make each 'point of contact' with your family, friends, and even strangers a point of mercy?

Objection of al-Shanti and the Two-Faced Answer
Razi reports al-Shanti's objection and begins the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Shanti's objection against the atomist proof from celestial circles and how the author begins to answer it using the two faces from the earlier proof.

Core Idea
Al-Shanti argues circles are continuous, so points are imaginary—yet the answer refutes him with the same two-faced argument.

This passage presents an objection by a mathematician named al-Shanti against the atomist proof using celestial circles. He argues that circles are continuous, so points only exist in imagination, not in reality. The author then begins the answer, referencing the two faces from the earlier proof. We will examine the objection and the initial response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation once to get the rhythm. Notice that the passage starts with 'And know that al-Shanti...' and cuts off mid-sentence.
The text has high OCR risk; the name 'al-Shanti' is uncertain.
2
Understand al-Shanti's objection
Al-Shanti says two circles intersect at points, but between points there are arcs and time. His evidence: the circle is a single line, points are only imaginary; when we imagine two points, they emerge from potency to act but a line must occur between them.
This challenges the atomist view that space and time are made of discrete points.
3
See the author's answer framework
The author says the answer is by the two faces mentioned in the earlier proof, and begins to elaborate the first face about contact on the arc.
The answer is incomplete in this passage; it only sets up the question.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection matters: it tries to show that continuous geometry cannot be explained by atoms. The author's response (when complete) would refute it using earlier arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن الشنتي المهندس ، عمل رسالة طويلة في الجواب عن هذا الكلام. وتطويله إنما حصل بسبب أنه ذكر دلائل نفاة الجوهر الفرد . وأما ما يكون جواباً عن هذا الحرف ، فلم يذكر إلا كلاماً واحداً : وهو أنه قال : و لا شك أن هاتين الدائرتين إنما يتلاقيان على نقطة واحدة ، ثم يتلاقيان على نقطة أخرى . إلا أن بين النقطتين : قوسان من تلك الدائرة ، وبين كل أنين : زمان . قال : والدليل عليه : أن الدائرة في نفسها : خط واحد . والنقطة : إنما ترتسم في الخط ، بسبب التوهم. فليس فيه شيء من النقط اصلا . فإذا توهمنا نقطة بعد نقطة أخرى ، فقد خرجت هاتان النقطتان من القوة إلى الفعل . إلا أنه لا بد وأن يحصل بينهما خط . والجواب عنه : بالوجهين المذكورين في البرهان المتقدم. ولا بأس بإعادتها . فتقول : أما الوجه الأول : فتقريره : أنه لما تقاطعت هاتان الدائرتان على نقطة . ثم تقاطعتا على النقطة الثانية . فإن فرضنا حصول قوس بينهما . فهل حصلت المماسة على تلك القوس
And know that al-Shanti the engineer composed a long treatise in response to this discussion. His lengthiness occurred only because he mentioned the proofs of the negators of the indivisible atom. As for what is a response to this particular point, he only mentioned one statement: He said: There is no doubt that these two circles only meet at one point, then they meet at another point, except that between the two points there are two arcs from that circle, and between each [meeting? there?] is time. He said: The evidence for it is that the circle in itself is a single line; the point is only imprinted in the line due to imagination. So there is no point in it at all. If we imagine a point after another point, then these two points have emerged from potency to actuality, but it is inevitable that a line occurs between them. The answer to it: by the two faces mentioned in the preceding proof. There is no harm in repeating them. So we say: As for the first face, its elaboration: When these two circles intersect at a point, then intersect at the second point, if we assume the occurrence of an arc between them, then did contact occur on that arc?

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from a certain engineer, al-Shanti (name uncertain), against the proof using celestial circles. When we imagine two points, they are potential, but a line must exist between them. The author then begins the answer, saying it is by the two faces mentioned in the earlier proof (the first proof). The answer is cut off, but the first face is restarted: after intersection at two points, if an arc is assumed between them, did contact occur on that arc? The answer is incomplete in the window.

Third Proof for the Atom: Defining the Point
Razi begins the third proof for the atom, defining the point as indivisible positioning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the starting definition of the third proof for the atom and Razi's commitment to demonstrative reasoning over dialectical argument.

Core Idea
The point is defined as indivisible position — the foundation for the atom's existence.

In this lesson, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi begins his third formal proof for the existence of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard). He starts by defining the key term 'point' (nuqta) and announces his method: he will provide a demonstrative proof, not merely a dialectical one. This passage sets the stage for the argument that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Let the author's own words set the foundation before analysis.
The passage is short but dense; read twice to absorb the definition and the methodological note.
2
Identify the definition of the point
Notice that Razi defines a point as 'a thing having position (waḍʿ) that is indivisible.' Ask: Why is indivisibility key?
This definition will be the premise for the proof of the atom.
3
See the connection to the atom
Razi says: if such points exist, then the atom is necessarily affirmed (lāzim). Understand that the atomic worldview depends on the reality of indivisible points.
The logical link is straightforward: atom = indivisible body, point = indivisible position.
4
Understand the methodological shift
Razi notes that the indivisibility of points is agreed among philosophers, but he intends to prove it demonstratively (burhānan) to move beyond dialectical (jadali) argument. Recognize the importance of this commitment in kalām.
This shows Razi's care for rigorous proof over mere consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثالث في إثبات الجوهر الفرد أن نقول : النقطة شيء، ذو وضع لا ينقسم . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، فالقول بإثبات الجوهر الفرد : لازم أما بيان الأول : فهو أمر متفق عليه بين جمهور الفلاسفة ، إلا أنا سنقيم البرهان على صحته ، ليصير الكلام برهانياً، ويخرج عن كونه جدلياً الزاميا .
The third proof for establishing the atom: That we say: The point is a thing having position (waḍʿ) that is indivisible. And when the matter is such, then the statement of establishing the atom is necessary. As for the demonstration of the first [that the point is indivisible], it is agreed upon among the majority of philosophers, except that we will establish the proof for its correctness in order to make the speech demonstrative (burhāniyyan) and to remove it from being dialectical (jadaliyyan) and obligating (ilzāmiyyan).
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage introduces a third formal proof for the existence of the atom. The proof begins with a definition: a point is an entity with position that is indivisible. If such points exist, then the atom (jawhar fard) is necessarily affirmed. The author notes that the indivisibility of points is agreed upon by most philosophers, but he intends to provide a proof for it to elevate the argument from mere dialectic (jadal) to demonstration (burhān). This shows the author's commitment to rigorous, demonstrative reasoning in kalam.

الجوهر الفرد (al-jawhar al-fard)
برهاني (burhānī)

The Point: Real Existence and Sensible Position
The author defines the point with three constraints and proves the first two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two constraints of the definition of a point: that it is a real existent thing and that it has a sensible position.

Core Idea
A point is not just a mental idea; it truly exists and can be pointed to.

In Islamic kalam, even the smallest things like points are defined with precision. This lesson unpacks the first two constraints of the definition of a point. The author proves that a point must exist in reality because lines meet at points, and that it has a position because its extremity can be sensed. This establishes the point as a real and locatable entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and constraints
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the author states the definition has three constraints, then explains the first two.
The third constraint is not covered in this passage.
2
Examine the proof for existence
Focus on the first constraint: 'it is a thing'. The proof is that lines meet at points. If points were non-existent, the meeting would be non-existence, which is irrational.
This refutes those who say points exist only in imagination.
3
Understand the second constraint
The second constraint is 'having position', meaning the point can be sensibly indicated. The author says this is self-evident from the fact that the end of a line can be pointed to.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Think about why these two constraints are necessary for defining a point. The author is establishing that points are real, not just mental constructs, and that they have a location.
This groundwork is important for later discussions in geometry and physics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : قولنا : النقطة شيء ذو وضع ، لا جزء لـه : كلام مشتمل على قيود ثلاثة :
القيد الأول : إنه شيء. والدليل عليه : أن الخط إنما يلقى الخط بالنقطة . فلو لم تكن النقطة موجودة ، لكان الموصوف بالملاقاة عدماً محضاً وإنه باطل في بديهة العقل . وبهذا يظهر فساد قول من يقول : النقطة لا وجود لها إلا في الوهم . لأنا نقول : الملاقاة حاصلة في الأعيان . والملاقاة إنما حصلت على النقطة ، فوجب كون النقطة موجودة في الأعيان
القيد الثاني : قولنا : إنها ذات وضع. والمراد منه : أنه يمكن الإشارة الحسية إليها . والعلم البديهي حاصل بأن طرف الخط ، يمكن الإشارة الحسية إليه
We say: Our statement 'the point is a thing possessing position, having no part' is speech comprising three constraints: The first constraint: that it is a thing. The proof of it: that a line meets another line only at a point. So if the point were not existent, then that which is described as meeting would be pure non-existence, and that is invalid by the first dictate of reason. And by this is shown the falsity of the saying of one who says: the point has no existence except in the imagination. For we say: meeting occurs in external objects, and meeting occurs only at the point, so it is necessary that the point exist in external objects. The second constraint: our statement that it possesses position, and what is meant by it is that sensible indication is possible toward it. And necessary knowledge is obtained that the extremity of a line can be sensibly indicated.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines the point (النقطة) by stating that it has three constraints (قيود). Here, it explains the first two. The first constraint is that the point is a thing (شيء), i.e., it has existence. The proof is that two lines meet only at a point. If the point were non-existent, the meeting would be a non-entity, which is absurd. This refutes those who say the point exists only in the imagination. The second constraint is that it has position (وضع), meaning it can be pointed at by the senses. This is self-evident from the fact that the end of a line can be sensed. The passage establishes that the point is real and sensible.

النقطة
شيء
وضع
ملاقاة
إشارة حسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim the point exists only in the imagination, not in external reality.
Correction: The author argues that if points were merely imaginary, then the meeting of two lines would be imaginary, which is false. Therefore points must exist externally.

Checkpoint

A) It can be imagined.
B) Lines meet at points.
C) It is indivisible.
D) It is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The point is a real existent thing (not merely imaginary) and has a sensible position, as proven by lines meeting at points and the ability to point to an extremity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In your own life, what invisible 'points'—small decisions or moments—have had real consequences because they were the meeting place of your intentions and actions? Are you neglecting these points, thinking they are insignificant? How will you be more mindful of them starting today?
?
The author argues that what is real must be capable of sensible indication. Your faith is not just an abstract idea; it should be pointed to in your actions. Is your faith evident in a way that others can 'sensibly indicate'—see, hear, or feel—in your daily behavior? What would they observe? Make a specific commitment to make one aspect of your faith visibly real this week.
?
If the point had no existence, the meeting of lines would be nonsense. Similarly, if your heart’s intentions are not sincere (real), your actions are empty. Examine your worship and dealings: are they built on the point of true sincerity, or are they mere appearances? Proofs of the Indivisibility of the Point
Presenting three proofs that the point is indivisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three logical proofs demonstrating that the geometric point is indivisible and how these proofs rely on the definition of the point as the extremity of a line.

Core Idea
The point is indivisible because it has no dimensions; any division would make it a line or surface.

The point is more than a mathematical fiction — for the theologians, it is a real existent that is sensibly perceptible. In this passage, the author presents three distinct proofs for its indivisibility, each examining the consequences of assuming division. The second and third proofs are particularly detailed, using the definition of the point as the extremity of a line.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first proof's reference
The first proof simply refers to four earlier proofs about the meeting-point on a sphere. This is not elaborated here, so note it as a reference.
You may need to consult earlier material for the full argument.
2
Analyze the second proof (dimensions argument)
The second proof examines the point as the extremity of a line. It uses a trichotomy: if the extremity has both length and width, it is a surface; if only length, it is a line; if neither, it is indivisible. Then it argues that if divisible, the parts would have quantity, creating length and width.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Study the third proof (regress argument)
The third proof assumes the extremity is divisible, leading to two halves. Neither half can be the extremity because the extremity is the other half. This leads to an infinite regress or a non-divisible terminus, proving the original point is indivisible.
This is a regress argument that terminates in the point's indivisibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقيد الثالث : إنه لا يقبل القسمة
والدليل عليه وجوه :
الأول : البراهين الأربعة المذكورة في بيان أن موضع الملاقاة من الكرة غير منقسم .
والثاني : إن النقطة عبارة عن طرف الخط . فنقول : طرف الخط . إن كان فيه طول وعرض ، فهو سطح . فلا يكون طرفاً للخط . وإن حصل فيه الطول فقط (۱) ، كان خطاً ، ولا يكون طرفاً للخط . وإن لم يحصل فيه لا طول ولا عرض ، كان غير منقسم البتة . إذ لو كان منقسماً ، لكان أحد قسميه مبايناً عن الآخر ، بحسب الإشارة الحسية. وحينئذ يكون مقدار ذلك المجموع ، أزيد من مقدار أحد نصفيه . وحينئذ يحصل (٢) الطول والعرض
والثالث : إن النقطة عبارة عن طرف الخط . فهذا الطرف . إن كان منقسماً ، حصل فيه نصفان ، وحينئذ لا يكون كل واحد من هذين النصفين ، طرفاً للخط ، بل طرف الخط ، هو النصف الآخر منه . وحينئذ الذي فرضناه بأنه طرف الخط ، ما كان طرفاً للخط . وذلك محال . ثم إن ذلك النصف . إن كان منقسماً عاد البحث فيه . وإن لم يكن منقسماً ، فهو المطلوب .
فثبت بهذه البيانات الظاهرة : أن النقطة شيء موجود ، مشار إليـه بحسب الحس ، غير منقسم .
The third constraint: that it does not accept division. Proofs of it are several: First: the four proofs mentioned in explaining that the meeting-place on the sphere is indivisible. Second: The point is the extremity of a line. So we say: the extremity of a line: if it has length and width, it is a surface, so it would not be an extremity of a line. If it has only length, it is a line, and it would not be an extremity of a line. If it has neither length nor width, it is absolutely indivisible. For if it were divisible, one of its two parts would be separate from the other with respect to sensible indication, and then the quantity of that sum would be greater than the quantity of one of its halves, and then length and width would occur. Third: The point is the extremity of a line. So this extremity: if it is divisible, it has two halves, and then each one of these two halves would not be an extremity of the line; rather the extremity of the line is the other half. And then what we supposed to be the extremity of the line would not be the extremity of the line, and that is impossible. Then if that half is divisible, the inquiry recurs; if it is not divisible, it is what is sought. So by these evident explanations it is established that the point is an existent thing, sensibly indicatable, indivisible.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents the third constraint of the point: indivisibility. It gives three proofs. The first proof references earlier proofs about the meeting-point on a sphere (not elaborated here). The second proof examines the point as the extremity of a line. If the extremity had length and width, it would be a surface; if only length, it would be a line; if neither, it is indivisible, because if divisible, it would have parts with quantity, thus length and width. The third proof is a regress argument: if the extremity is divisible, then its half is not the extremity; the extremity is the other half, leading to infinite regress or a non-divisible terminus. Hence the point is indivisible. The conclusion reaffirms the three attributes: existence, sensible perceptibility, and indivisibility.

Key Terms
النقطة
غير منقسم
طرف الخط
براهين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The indivisibility proof relies on imagination or physical intuition.
Correction: The proofs are purely logical, based on definitions and reductio arguments, not on imagining the point as a physical object.

Checkpoint
In the second proof, if the extremity of a line has only length, what is it?
A) A surface
B) A point
C) A line
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
Through logical analysis of the point as the extremity of a line, the author demonstrates its indivisibility, establishing it as a real, sensibly indicatable entity without parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the point is an existent thing, sensibly indicatable yet indivisible. In your life, where have you accepted 'division' in matters of faith—splitting your loyalty between Allah and your desires—without realizing the contradiction? What would it mean to treat your submission as indivisible starting today?
?
The proof shows that infinite divisibility leads to logical collapse. Consider your own intentions: have you convinced yourself that small, repeated compromises will not add up? How does the reality of indivisible actions—each deed standing alone before Allah—change your view of even the smallest decision you are about to make?
?
The point is an extremity, an endpoint. In your spiritual journey, have you recognized that your life has an endpoint—an indivisible moment when deeds cease? How does the certainty of that indivisible end compel you to stop delaying repentance and start acting now?

The Point is a Substance, Not an Accident
Two proofs that the point is a substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational proofs that the indivisible point must be a substance (جوهر) and cannot be an accident (عرض).

Core Idea
The point cannot be an accident because that leads to infinite regress or overlapping magnitudes.

After establishing that the point exists and is indivisible, the author now asks: is it a substance or an accident? The two proofs that follow eliminate the possibility of it being an accident, forcing the conclusion that it is a substance, specifically the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد). This lesson unpacks those proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's argument flow through you.
Notice that the passage begins with a conditional structure: 'If it were an accident, then...'.
2
Understand the first proof: infinite regress
The first proof argues that an accident requires a locus. If that locus is divisible, the point becomes divisible. If the locus is indivisible but still an accident, it needs another locus, leading to an infinite chain, which is impossible. Thus, the chain must end in a self-subsistent indivisible substance.
This proof relies on the impossibility of infinite regress (تسلسل) in metaphysical arguments.
3
Understand the second proof: overlapping magnitudes
The second proof argues that a pointed thing occupies a specific space. If it had a locus, that locus would either also occupy that space (causing overlapping of magnitudes) or not. The second option is impossible because an accident that is not specific cannot give specificity to something else. Hence, the point cannot be an accident.
This proof uses the principle that an accident cannot transfer a property it does not possess.
4
See the convergence of both proofs
Both proofs independently show that the point cannot be an accident. Therefore, it must be a substance, specifically the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد) that is the cornerstone of kalam atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : هذا الشيء . إما أن يكون جوهراً أو عرضاً. وباطل أن يكون عرضاً ، فبقي أن يكون جوهراً . ولا معنى للجوهر الفرد إلا ذلك
►
وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون عرضاً لوجهين :
الأول : إنه [ إن (۱) ] كان عرضاً ، فلا بد له من محل . فذلك المحل إما أن يكون منقسماً ، أو غير منقسم . والأول باطل . لأن الحال في المنقسم منقسم ، فيلزم كون النقطة منقسمة ، بسبب انقسام محلها . وذلك محال . فبقي أن يقال : إن محل النقطة شيء غير منقسم . ثم نعيد التقسيم فيه وهو أن ذلك الشيء . إن كان عرضاً افتقر إلى محل آخر . فإما أن يذهب هذا الحكم إلى غير النهاية ، وهو محال . أو ينتهي إلى محل قائم بالنفس ، غير قابل للقسمة . وذلك هو الجوهر الفرد
الثاني : وهو إن كل ما كان مشاراً إليه. فإنه يكون مختصاً بذلك الحيز المعين ويكون حاصلاً في تلك الجهة المعينة. فإن فرضنا له محلاً وموصوفاً وقابلاً ، فذلك المحل . إما أن يكون مختصاً بذلك الحيز المعين ، وإما أن لا يكون . فإن كان الأول ، فحينئذ يلزم تداخل المقادير . وإن كان الثاني فهو محال . لأن حلول ما لا يكون مختصاً بالحيز والجهة في شيء ، يمتنع أن يكون له اختصاص [ بالحيز والجهة (۲) ] مدفوع في بديهة العقل
And when this is established, we say: This thing is either a substance or an accident. It is invalid that it be an accident, so it remains that it be a substance. And there is no meaning to the indivisible substance (al-jawhar al-fard) except that. And we said that it is not permissible that it be an accident for two reasons: First: If it were an accident, it would necessarily have a locus (mahall). That locus is either divisible or indivisible. The first is invalid, because that which inheres in a divisible thing is divisible, so the point would be divisible due to the division of its locus, and that is impossible. So it remains to say that the locus of the point is something indivisible. Then we repeat the division on it: that thing, if it were an accident, would need another locus, so either this judgment goes on infinitely, which is impossible, or it ends at a locus subsisting by itself, not admitting division, and that is the indivisible substance. Second: [It is] that everything that is pointed toward is particularized by that determinate space and occurs in that specific direction. If we suppose for it a locus, a described subject, and a receptacle, that locus either is particularized by that determinate space or not. If the first, then the overlapping of magnitudes would follow. If the second, it is impossible, because the indwelling of that which is not particularized by space and direction in a thing prevents it from having particularization by space and direction, and this is rejected by the first dictate of reason.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage concludes that the point must be a substance (جوهر), specifically the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد). After establishing that the point exists, is sensible, and indivisible, the author asks whether it is a substance or an accident. It cannot be an accident because of two proofs. First: an accident requires a locus (محل). If that locus is divisible, the point would be divisible, which is already ruled out. If the locus is indivisible, we repeat the question: if that indivisible locus is itself an accident, it would need another locus, leading to infinite regress, which is impossible. Therefore, we must end at a locus that is self-subsistent (قائم بالنفس) and indivisible—that is the indivisible substance (الجوهر الفرد). Second: Anything that is sensibly indicated occupies a specific space and direction. If we suppose it has a locus, that locus either is also specific to that space (leading to overlapping of magnitudes) or is not specific (impossible, because the accident would then inherit specificity from a locus that has no specificity, which is absurd). Hence, the point cannot be an accident; it must be a substance.

عرض
الجوهر الفرد
محل
قائم بالنفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument relies on empirical observation of points or atoms.
Correction: The proofs are purely rational (metaphysical) arguments based on the concepts of divisibility, locus, and infinite regress, not on sensory evidence.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that the point cannot be an accident?
A) Accidents must be perceptible.
B) Accidents require a locus, and the locus would lead to infinite regress.
C) Accidents are divisible.
D) Points are not located.

Key Takeaway
The point must be a self-subsistent substance (جوهر) because being an accident would lead either to infinite regress or to impossible overlapping of magnitudes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson argues that the point must be a self-subsistent substance because otherwise we would face infinite regress. Reflect: In your own life, do you ever rely on rational arguments to believe in something you cannot see or touch? What does that reveal about your trust in reason over sensory experience?
?
The author insists that the point is a real entity, not just a mental abstraction. Think about something you have always considered 'just an idea'—like justice, or the self, or your own soul. Based on the reasoning here, could it actually be a real substance? How does that challenge the way you think about your own existence?

Objection and Answer on Inherence of a State in a Composite
The author refutes an objection about the divisibility of a state inhering in a composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument about state inherence in a composite and the fallacy of confusing the whole with its parts.

Core Idea
Inherence requires existence in the parts; no part, no whole.

This passage tackles a subtle fallacy in kalam: the assumption that a state might inhere in a composite without inhering in any part. The author clarifies that inherence means existence in the locus, so if the locus is divisible, the state must be divided among parts. He then defends this against an objection and adds a brief note on unity, relation, and finitude.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three possibilities
Read the passage to identify the three ways a state could relate to a composite's parts: no inherence, entire state in each part, or part of state in each part.
2
Understand the refutation of the first option
Notice why the author deems the first option impossible: if no part has the state, the composite cannot have it—this is self-evident.
This relies on the necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī).
3
Grasp the fallacy
The objector's counter-argument tries to separate whole from parts. The author explains the fallacy: the composite is not identical to its parts individually.
The key distinction: 'composite of the thing insofar as it is that composite' vs. 'each one of the parts.'
4
Consider the extra points
Finally, note the author's rejection of unity, relation, and finitude as real attributes, arguing they lead to regress or are merely privations.
This is a separate argument attached to the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجزاء ذلك المحل . لا تمام ذلك الحال ، ولا شيء من أجزاء ذلك الحال . أو يقال : إنه قام تمام ذلك الحال ، بكل واحد من أجزاء ذلك المحل . أو يقال : إنه قام بكل واحد من أجزاء ذلك المحل : جزء من أجزاء ذلك الحال . والأول باطل . لأنه إذا لم يقم بشيء من أجزاء ذلك المحل . لاتمام ذلك الحال ولا شيء من أجزاء ذلك الحال : امتنع وصف ذلك الشيء بتلك الصفة . والعلم به ضروري. والثاني يفيد انقسام الحال ، بسبب انقسام المحل . وهو المطلوب . لأنا نقول : لم قلتم : إنه لما لم يتصف شيء من أجزاء المحل . لا تمام ذلك الحال ، ولا بشيء من أجزائه ، فإنه يمتنع كون ذلك المجموع موصوفاً بتلك الصفة ؟ وتقريره : إن مجموع الشيء ، مغاير لكل واحد . من أجزائه وأبعاضه . فلم قلتم : إنه يلزم من خلو كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك الموصوف ، عن كل تلك الصفة ، وعن بعض من أبعاضها : خلو مجموع ذلك الموصوف عن تلك الصفة ؟ فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن هذه المغالطة إنما وقعت ، لأجل الغفلة عن الفرق بين مجموع الشيء من حيث إنه ذلك المجموع ، وبين كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك المجموع . والجواب : إنه لا معنى لحلول الشيء في الشيء ، إلا كونه حاصلاً فيه. فإذا كان ذلك المحل منقسماً ، فلا معنى لوجود ذلك الشيء ، إلا تلك الأقسام والأبعاض . فإن كان كل واحد من تلك الأقسام والأبعاض ، خالياً عن تلك الصفة . كان العلم الضروري حاصلاً بامتناع كون ذلك المجموع ، موصوفاً بتلك الصفة . وأما الوحدة والإضافة والنهاية ، فلا نسلم أنها أمور موجودة إذ لو كانت الوحدة صفة موجودة لكان كل فرد من أفرادها وحدة. ولزم التسلسل . وكذلك يلزم التسلسل في الإضافات . وأما النهاية فمعناها انقطاع ذلك الشيء وفناؤه . وذلك عدم .
The parts of that locus, not the entirety of that state, nor any part of that state. Or it is said: that the entirety of that state inheres in each part of that locus. Or it is said: that in each part of that locus inheres a part of the parts of that state. The first is false, because if it does not inhere in any part of that locus, neither the entirety of that state nor any part of that state, then it is impossible for that thing to be described by that attribute, and knowledge of it is necessary. The second implies the division of the state due to the division of the locus, and that is the desired conclusion. For we say: Why do you claim that when no part of the locus is qualified by either the entirety of that state or any part of its parts, then it is impossible for that composite to be described by that attribute? And its clarification: The composite of a thing is different from each one of its parts and portions. So why do you claim that from the emptiness of each one of the parts of that described thing of all of that attribute and of some of its parts, it follows that the composite of that described thing is empty of that attribute? So it is established by what we have mentioned that this fallacy occurred only because of neglect of the distinction between the composite of a thing insofar as it is that composite, and each one of the parts of that composite. And the answer: There is no meaning for the inherence of a thing in a thing except that it exists in it. So if that locus is divisible, there is no meaning for the existence of that thing except those divisions and parts. If each one of those divisions and parts is empty of that attribute, then necessary knowledge obtains that it is impossible for that composite to be described by that attribute. As for unity, relation, and finitude, we do not concede that they are existing things. For if unity were an existing attribute, then every individual of its individuals would be a unity, and an infinite regress would follow. Similarly, an infinite regress would follow in relations. As for finitude, its meaning is the cessation and annihilation of that thing, and that is non-existence.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the inherence of a state (ḥāl) in a composite (murakkab) subject. The objector posits three possibilities: either the state inheres in the parts not at all, or the entire state inheres in each part, or each part contains a part of the state. The author rejects the first as impossible because then the composite could not be described by that state, which is evidently false. The second option implies the state is divided with the locus, which is what the author wants to prove. The objector then challenges: why must the composite be empty of the attribute if each part lacks it? The author clarifies the fallacy: the objector confuses the composite as a whole with its parts individually. The answer reiterates that inherence means existence in the locus; if the locus is divisible, the existing thing is divided among its parts. If no part has the attribute, the composite cannot have it—this is self-evident. The author then digresses to deny that unity, relation, and finitude are real attributes, arguing that unity would lead to infinite regress, relation has the same problem, and finitude is merely privation.

محل (maḥall)

أجزاء (ajzāʾ)
انقسام (inqisām)
وحدة (waḥda)
إضافة (iḍāfa)
نهاية (nihāya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A state can inhere in a composite whole even if no part of the composite has that state.
Correction: Inherence means existence in the locus; if the locus is divisible, the state must exist in its parts. If no part has it, the whole cannot have it.

Checkpoint

A) Because the composite is identical to its parts.
B) Because inherence means existence in the locus, and if the locus is divisible, the state must exist in its parts.
C) Because the state is indivisible.
D) Because the objector's argument is invalid due to lack of evidence.

Key Takeaway
The fallacy of confusing the composite with its parts is central; inherence requires existence in the parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may think your faith (īmān) is a solid whole, but does it actually exist in the parts of your daily actions—your prayers, your speech, your dealings? If some parts are empty of it, are you deluding yourself that the whole remains intact? Name one part that is lacking and commit today to restoring it.
?
The author denies that unity is a real attribute. Do you treat your own self as a simple, unchanging unity, ignoring the composite reality of your thoughts, intentions, and deeds? How might that false unity excuse you from accountability for each part? Examine one fragmented part of your life that you have been ignoring as 'not really you'.
?
You likely believe that a state can inhere in a composite without the parts—like 'being healthy' without each organ being healthy. Has this fallacy crept into your spiritual life: assuming overall piety while neglecting specific obligations? Pick one neglected obligation and make a concrete plan to fulfill it.

Fourth Proof for the Indivisible Atom from Motion
A proof for the indivisible atom from motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof for the indivisible atom based on the beginning and end of motion, and why the place of motion's start and end must be indivisible.

Core Idea
The start and end of motion must be at indivisible points, proving the indivisible atom.

This passage presents a crucial proof for atomism in classical Islamic theology (kalam). By analyzing the spatial location where motion begins and ends, the author argues that these locations cannot be divisible. This leads to the necessity of indivisible atoms (jawhar fard). We'll walk through the logical steps of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the author's concise style.
2
Identify the key claim
The central claim is that the place where motion begins and ends is indivisible. See how the author proves this.
3
Examine the divisibility refutation
Focus on why divisible places are impossible: they would require the moving body to be in two places at once.
This is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الرابع في إثبات الجوهر الفرد : أن نقول : الموضع الذي يحصل فيه مبدأ الحركة ومنتهاها ، غير منقسم . وإذا ثبت هذا ، كان القول بالجوهر الفرد لازماً . أما إثبات المقام الأول . فالدليل عليه : أن المتحرك ، إذا ابتدأ بالحركة : فالموضع الذي يحصل فيه ابتداء الحركة. إما أن يكون منقسماً ، أو غير منقسم . والأول باطل . لأن كل منقسم ، فإنه يفترض فيه نصفان . ومن المحتمل أن يقع ابتداء الحركة في كلا النصفين ، وإلا لكان طرف المتحرك ونهايته واقعاً في كلا النصفين، فيلزم حصول الشيء الواحد في مكانين دفعة وإنه محال . وإذا بطل هذا ، ثبت : أن الذي يقع فيه ابتداء الحركة ، لا بد وأن يكون شيئاً غير منقسم . فثبت : أن الموضع الذي يقع فيه ابتداء الحركة ، ويقع فيه انتهاؤها ، يجب أن يكون شيئاً غير منقسم . وإذا ثبت هذا ، وجب أن يكون القول بالجوهر الفرد لازماً ، للوجهين المذكورين في الدليل المتقدم . والله أعلم .
The fourth proof for establishing the indivisible atom: that we say: The place where the beginning and end of motion occur is indivisible. And if this is established, then the assertion of the indivisible atom is necessary. As for establishing the first point, the proof for it: that when the moving body begins motion, the place where the beginning of motion occurs is either divisible or indivisible. The first is false, because every divisible thing is assumed to have two halves. It is possible that the beginning of motion occurs in both halves; otherwise, the extremity and end of the moving body would be located in both halves, which entails that one thing obtains in two places at once, and that is impossible. And if this is invalidated, it is established that that in which the beginning of motion occurs must be something indivisible. So it is established that the place where the beginning of motion occurs and where its end occurs must be something indivisible. And when this is established, the assertion of the indivisible atom becomes necessary, for the two aspects mentioned in the preceding proof. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for the existence of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard), based on the analysis of motion. The argument is structured as follows: The place where motion begins and ends must be indivisible. If that is proven, then atomism follows. The proof for the indivisibility: when a moving body starts moving, the location of the start of motion is either divisible or indivisible. Divisible is false because if it were divisible, it would have two halves, and it is possible that the start of motion occurs in both halves (otherwise the moving body's extremity would be in both halves simultaneously, which is impossible). Thus the location must be indivisible. The same applies to the end of motion. Therefore, the positions of start and end are indivisible points, which implies the existence of indivisible atoms. The author also refers to 'two aspects mentioned in the preceding proof'—likely the idea that if motion occurs over divisible space, it would involve infinite divisibility and absurdities—thus the necessity of atoms is reaffirmed.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد (jawhar fard)
مبدأ الحركة (mabdaʾ al-ḥaraka)
منتهاها (muntahāhā)
انقسام (inqisām)
متحرك (mutaḥarrik)
نصفان (niṣfān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof requires motion to be discrete, but the argument is about the spatial location of the start of motion, not the continuity of motion itself.
Correction: The proof does not assume that motion itself is discontinuous; it only analyzes the spatial point where motion begins, which must be indivisible to avoid contradictory consequences.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the possibility that the place where motion begins is divisible?
A) Because divisibility leads to an infinite number of places.
B) Because it would imply that the moving body's extremity is in two places at once, which is impossible.
C) Because motion cannot begin in a divisible space.
D) Because the moving body would have to overlap with itself.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof shows that the spatial points where motion starts and ends must be indivisible, thus supporting the existence of the indivisible atom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you begin any action—whether praying, speaking, or moving—the very start occurs at an indivisible point. Have you ever considered that Allah witnesses that precise moment? Will you now pause before every action to ensure it begins with His remembrance?
?
The proof refutes the idea that your movements can be divided infinitely. This means your life is composed of finite, countable moments. Are you living each moment as if it counts, knowing that each start and end is seen by Allah?
?
If it is impossible for one thing to be in two places at once, then you cannot serve two masters. Which master are you serving in the indivisible moments of your day? What will you change today?

Proof that Motion Requires an Indivisible Place
Proof that the place of motion's beginning and end must be indivisible, implying the atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that motion's beginning and end must occur in an indivisible location, necessitating the existence of the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
Motion itself proves that space is made of indivisible atoms.

The kalam tradition built a powerful argument for atomism from the phenomenon of motion. The author argues that when a body begins to move, the place where motion starts cannot be divisible. If it were divisible, the body would have to be in two places at once—an impossibility. This forces us to conclude that space is composed of indivisible units, which are the atoms. In this lesson, we will closely examine this proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical flow: thesis, proof, conclusion.
2
Identify the key claim
Identify the main claim: the place of motion's beginning and end is indivisible. Understand why this leads to the atom.
3
Walk through the proof step by step
Trace the reasoning: location is either divisible or not. Divisible leads to impossibility because one thing cannot be in two places at once. Therefore, indivisible.
The proof uses a conditional (if divisible then impossible) and modus tollens.
4
Reflect on why divisibility leads to impossibility
Consider why a divisible location forces the moving body to be in two halves simultaneously, which is absurd. This is the core insight.
This is a classic kalam argument against infinite divisibility of space.
5
Connect to atomism
Understand that the indivisible location is an atom (jawhar fard). The proof shows that space itself is atomic.
This is one of the main proofs for atomism in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيه مبدأ الحركة ومنتهاها ، غير منقسم . وإذا ثبت هذا ، كان القول بالجوهر الفرد لازماً . أما إثبات المقام الأول . فالدليل عليه : أن المتحرك ، إذا ابتدأ بالحركة : فالموضع الذي يحصل فيه ابتداء الحركة. إما أن يكون منقسماً ، أو غير منقسم . والأول باطل . لأن كل منقسم ، فإنه يفترض فيه نصفان . ومن المحتمل أن يقع ابتداء الحركة في كلا النصفين ، وإلا لكان طرف المتحرك ونهايته واقعاً في كلا النصفين، فيلزم حصول الشيء الواحد في مكانين دفعة وإنه محال . وإذا بطل هذا ، ثبت : أن الذي يقع فيه ابتداء الحركة ، لا بد وأن يكون شيئاً غير منقسم . فثبت : أن الموضع الذي يقع فيه ابتداء الحركة ، ويقع فيه انتهاؤها ، يجب أن يكون شيئاً غير منقسم . وإذا ثبت هذا ، وجب أن يكون القول بالجوهر الفرد لازماً ، للوجهين المذكورين في الدليل المتقدم . والله أعلم .
In it (the place) is the beginning of motion and its end, indivisible. And if this is established, then the assertion of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) is necessary. As for establishing the first stage, the proof for it is: that the moving body, when it begins motion, the location in which the beginning of motion occurs is either divisible or indivisible. The first is false, because every divisible thing is assumed to have two halves. It is possible that the beginning of motion occurs in both halves; otherwise, the extremity and end of the moving body would occur in both halves, necessitating that one thing occurs in two places at once, which is impossible. And when this is refuted, it is established that that in which the beginning of motion occurs must be something indivisible. So it is established that the location in which the beginning of motion occurs and in which its end occurs must be something indivisible. And when this is established, the assertion of the indivisible atom is necessarily required, for the two aspects mentioned in the preceding proof. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a proof from motion for the existence of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), a key doctrine in classical kalam atomism. The author begins by stating that the place (al-mawdi‘) in which motion begins and ends is indivisible. If this is proven, then the doctrine of the atom follows necessarily. The proof for the first point—that the starting place of motion is indivisible—is given: a moving body, when it starts moving, occurs in some location. That location is either divisible or indivisible. Divisible is impossible, because a divisible thing has two halves. If the body starts in the first half, it cannot simultaneously be at the border between halves without being in two places. Alternatively, if it starts in both halves at once, then the body's extremity would be in both halves simultaneously, implying one thing occupies two places at once—an impossibility. Therefore the starting place must be indivisible. By extension, the ending place is also indivisible. Thus the atom is necessary, as per two aspects mentioned in an earlier proof (likely the impossibility of infinite regress or composition). The conclusion is affirmed with 'And God knows best.'

Key Terms
جوهر فرد (indivisible atom)
حركة (motion)
مبدأ الحركة (beginning of motion)
منتهى الحركة (end of motion)
منقسم (divisible)
غير منقسم (indivisible)
نصفان (two halves)
محال (impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof applies only to physical places; in kalam it applies to space itself, which is composed of indivisible atoms.
Correction: The proof is about the nature of space: any location where motion occurs must be an indivisible point, i.e., an atom.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why can the location of motion's beginning not be divisible?
A) Because a divisible place would have multiple parts, causing the moving body to be in two places simultaneously.
B) Because motion cannot occur in a divisible place.
C) Because the moving body must occupy the entire place at once.
D) Because the atom is too small to be seen.

Key Takeaway
The starting and ending points of motion must be indivisible, which proves the existence of the indivisible atom.

In what areas of your life have you been subtly associating partners with Him (shirk) by giving His rights to others? Commit to identifying one such area and correcting it.

Defending the Proof Against Two Key Objections
Two objections and the author's logical response (cut off)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends his first proof for the impossibility of infinite division against objections that reinterpret 'infinite division' as merely potential or restrict it to imaginary division.

Core Idea
Each division is possible; therefore all together are possible — logical compatibility overrides philosophical doctrines.

In this passage, the author anticipates two sophisticated objections to his earlier proof. The first objection clarifies that philosophers never claimed all infinite divisions exist simultaneously; they only mean the process can continue indefinitely. The second objection introduces Democritus' distinction between real and imaginary division. The author's response is a masterclass in logical reasoning: he shows that his argument rests on the simple principle that if each division is individually possible and not mutually exclusive, then their simultaneous existence is logically necessary — regardless of what any philosopher intended.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first objection
Read the objector's claim: philosophers meant division can continue indefinitely, not that all divisions coexist. They use the analogy of God's power (successive creation) and numbers (each number possible but the infinite set impossible). Consider how this might seem to undermine the proof.
The objector is trying to show that the proof's conclusion does not follow if infinite division is only potential.
2
Study the second objection from Democritus
Democritus argues that bodies reduce to ultimate parts that are only imaginarily divisible, not actually separable. This avoids the need for an actual infinite of parts. Identify the key terms: 'al-qisma al-infikākiyya' (actual separation) vs 'al-qisma al-wahmiyya' (imaginary division).
Democritus' view challenges the proof by denying that infinite divisions can ever become actual.
3
Examine the author's logical response
The author states his proof does not depend on any philosopher's specific statement. Instead, it relies on the principle: if each of an infinite set of divisions is possible and none prevents another, then their combination in one body is possible. This leads to the absurd conclusion that a finite body would have an infinite number of actual parts.
The author reframes the debate from 'what philosophers said' to 'what logic requires'.
4
Reflect on the incomplete ending
The passage ends mid-sentence while addressing the analogy of God's power. Consider what the full response might say: likely that God's power pertains to successive creation, not simultaneous infinite actuality, whereas the divisions in a body would be simultaneous.
The missing part is probably a refutation of the analogy by highlighting the difference between potential and actual infinite.

If it is said: The philosophers did not intend by their statement 'the body is capable of infinite divisions' that it is valid for all those divisions to enter into existence. Rather, they intended: that the body does not reach a limit of smallness except that after that it is capable of division. And this does not entail that all those divisions are possible of obtaining. Just as the theologians, when they said 'He, exalted, is powerful over an infinite number of possible things', they did not thereby mean that He can create an infinite number of possible things, but they meant: that He does not reach a limit in creating possible things except that He can bring into existence and create after that, meaning that He can bring into existence after bringing into existence and create after create, while each thing that emerges into actuality is finite. So likewise here. Also, each individual rank of numbers is possible of obtaining, but the sum of ranks that are infinite is not possible of obtaining. So why should it not be that it is similar here? The second question: Grant that what you mentioned is necessary for Aristotle and Avicenna, who said that 'intermittent division (al-qisma al-infikākiyya) is possible of obtaining ad infinitum', but it is not necessary for Democritus and his followers. For they said: 'These sensible bodies, their compound reaches in analysis ultimate parts that are capable of imaginary division ad infinitum but are not capable of actual separation. By this estimation the aforementioned difficulty is repelled.' And the answer: We say: We did not build our proof on their statement. Rather, we say: Since each one of those infinite divisions is possible, and the existence of some does not prevent the rest, it is necessary that their sum is possible in the manner of togetherness. And then the aforementioned difficulty follows. So the philosophers, whether they said it or did not say it, the implication is binding upon them, with no escape for them from it. As for His being powerful over...

Commentary
This passage contains two objections to the first proof and the author's response. The first objection clarifies that philosophers meant only that division can continue indefinitely in sequence, not that all divisions can occur simultaneously. They analogize with the theologians' statement about God's power: God can create endlessly but never an actual infinite. Also, like numbers: each number is possible but the infinite sum is not possible. The second objection from Democritus claims that bodies have ultimate parts not capable of actual separation, only imaginary division, thus avoiding the conclusion. The author responds that his proof does not rely on the philosophers' specific views but on the logical principle that if each division is individually possible and non-exclusive, then all together are possible. This forces the conclusion regardless of the philosophers' stance. The answer is cut off at the end of the page, but it continues addressing the analogy about God's power.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author's proof is refuted by the philosophers' clarification that they meant potential division, or by Democritus' distinction between imaginary and actual division.
Correction: The author shows that even potential infinite divisions imply the possibility of simultaneous infinite if each is individually possible and compatible. The proof is based on logical possibility, not on any particular philosophical doctrine.

First Argument: Matter Is Not the Entire Essence of Body
First argument that matter is only part of the body's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that matter (hayula) is only a component of a body's essence, not the entire essence, and that this distinction is necessary for reasoning about the body's identity and continuance.

Core Idea
Matter is part of a body's essence, not its entirety.

In this lesson, we examine the first of several arguments concerning the nature of body. The author begins by clarifying the role of matter (hayula) within the essence (mahiyya). This seemingly simple definition has important logical consequences for understanding what it means for a thing to continue existing. The argument is concise, but it sets the stage for deeper discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the English translation. Notice the concise style.
The passage contains a single logical argument.
2
Identify key terms
Identify the three key terms: هيولى (matter), ماهية (essence), بقاء (continuance). Understand their meanings in context.
These terms are foundational for kalam discussions of body.
3
Analyze logical structure
Break the argument into its components: definition (matter is part of essence), principle (continuance of part is insufficient), and conclusion (the definition is necessary).
The argument uses a deductive structure.
4
Reflect on necessity
Consider why the author says the claim is 'necessary'. How does the principle support the definition?
The necessity arises from the logical link between the definition and the principle.
